*   **File Obfuscation:** Mentions of "криптованный свежий билд" (encrypted fresh build),`undll32 file.dll, Museum` and the use of "3 крипта ехе и длл каждого шк" (3 crypts for exe and dll each) indicates attempts to evade detection by antivirus and EDR solutions through encryption and obfuscation of malicious files.`1.7z`is likely used as the container.

*   **Process Injection:** Mentions of "injection в процесс, который уже запущен"(injection into a process that is already running) suggests techniques for injecting malicious code into legitimate processes to evade detection.

*   **Process Identification and Termination:** The code snippets involving `tasklist`, `wmic process where "CommandLine like '%.vbs%' and name != 'wmic.exe'" get /value` and `так найдешь айди процесса потом килл` (so you will find the process ID and then kill) are used to identify and terminate processes. It shows techniques for finding specific processes (script.exe or those with VBS in their command line) and then killing them, possibly to disable security tools or remove traces of activity. This is an EDR killer technique.

*   **Anti-Analysis Techniques:** The phrase "anti analysis environment" refers to measures taken to detect and evade analysis by sandboxes, reverse engineering tools, or security researchers. Specific anti-analysis environment mentions installing an "anti vm". Also mentions an existing implementation in use, "Этот комодо любое приложение в контейнер заворачивает".

*   **Rootkit usage:** Mentions a "rootkit" and the use of "spoff" and 'hide' which is used for making files persistent while avoiding detection.

*   **DLL sideloading:** the `file.dll` name implies this.

    *   `@w:matrixtcFJHPDblmt2rg.network: я выдал лапе если тчо уже / все чисто` - Indicating delivery to `@lapa` has been done and everything looks clean.

*   **Shellcode Injection:** The chat discusses injecting shellcode into running processes as a method for executing malicious code.
    *   `@w:matrixtcFJHPDblmt2rg.network: инжект можно сделать пару способами: 1. Создание нового потока в этом процессе 2. Инжект в DLL левую в самом процессе` - Discussing two shellcode injection techniques: creating a new thread or injecting into a DLL within the target process.
    *   `@w:matrixtcFJHPDblmt2rg.network: инжектится шелкод / да, инжектиться шелкод` - Confirming shellcode is the injected payload.
    *   `@w:matrixtcFJHPDblmt2rg.network: это же обычные байты, которые можно запихнуть в DLL процесса в entrypoint` - Describing shellcode as raw bytes that can be placed into a DLL's entry point.

*   **Crypter Use:** While not explicitly detailing crypter usage, there's a clear need for a crypter to further evade AV detection.
    *   `@usernameyy:matrix.bestflowers247.online: я гружу, чем криптовать будете? / @usernamess:matrix.bestflowers247.online: ничем` - The group is lacking a crypter at the moment.

    *   `@usernamess:matrix.bestflowers247.online: там палится все стабы / да он и не криптует` - The tool in discussion doesn't have crypter features.

**Summary of Techniques and Methods:**

*   **Fileless Techniques:** The use of shellcode injection suggests an attempt to minimize writing files to disk, reducing the chances of detection by file-based AV scans.
*   **Process Injection:**  Injecting shellcode into legitimate processes (via new thread creation or DLL injection) can help to hide malicious activity by running code within the context of a trusted process.
*   **Obfuscation:** VBS scripts and LNK files may be used in conjunction with other obfuscation techniques to further conceal the malicious payload.
*   **Dynamic Code Generation:** The fact that they are generating VBS scripts suggests they are using some level of dynamic code generation, which makes it harder for AV signatures to detect their malware.
*   **IP rotation/cleaning**: Attempts to solve the issue of static IPs in AV databases.
*   **"Bypassing" detections with crypters** The crypter is used as an AV bypass technique.

**Summary of Techniques, Tools, and Methods Discussed:**

*   **String Obfuscation:** Breaking up static strings, particularly IP addresses, to avoid signature-based detection.
*   **Code Randomization and Rebuilding:** Dynamically generating or altering malware code (e.g., rebuilding the "skeleton") to evade detection.
*   **Encryption:** Encrypting the loader/payloads to avoid static analysis.
*   **Process Injection:** Injecting malicious code into legitimate processes to hide its activity (mentions explorer.exe, svchost.exe).
*   **Parent Process Spoofing:** Faking the parent process of a malicious process to further conceal its origin.
*   **Module Stomping:** A specific process injection technique.
*   **Dynamic API Resolution (Implied):**  The discussion regarding Win 11 API changes (syscall numbers) implies they are using dynamic API resolution to avoid relying on static imports that could be detected.
*    **Userland Hooking Evasion:** It can be implied that this group is aware of and potentially uses EDR evasion techniques centered on evading userland hooks. This can be seen with the discussion on migrating "back to base" by doing a shellcode injection over rundll32.
*   **Network-Level Evasion:** Rotating IPs, using proxy bots, and potentially using DNS for C2 communications to avoid network-based detection/blocking.
*   **Payload Chaining/Staging:** Using LNK files to download and execute payloads (DLLs, VBS scripts) from remote servers.
*   **File Type Masquerading:**  Discussion around using different file types (VBS, XLL, attaching a file to a PDF with a .lnk) as means to evade user and/or host detection
*   **Rootkit/HVNC Usage (Implied):** "скрытый" vnc implies using techniques, likely hvnc, to hide active vnc sessions from the user
*   **Hiding Proxies :** The group often rotates proxy IPs.
*   **Defense Layering:** This group of threat actors has multiple points of defense to help avoid detections

[2023-10-01]
----------
The following content from the chat log relates to EDR evasion and Antivirus bypasses:

*   **"мне надо дроппер чистый" ("I need a clean dropper"):** This indicates the need for a dropper that is not detected by antivirus or other security solutions. A "clean" dropper implies it can bypass initial detection mechanisms.

*   **"вроде надо криптовать" ("seems like it needs to be encrypted"):** Suggests the use of encryption to obfuscate the malicious payload and avoid detection by signature-based antivirus or other security products.

*   **"лучше бы он сделал это ввиже инжектирования бинарного шелл кода в процесс" ("it would be better if he did it in the form of injecting binary shellcode into a process"):** This explicitly discusses process injection, a common technique used to evade detection by running malicious code within a legitimate process. This can bypass some EDR and antivirus solutions that monitor for suspicious process creation.

*   **"и перезальем хвнц" ("and re-upload hvnc"):** While not explicitly an evasion technique, regularly re-uploading tools like HVNC (Hidden Virtual Network Computing) could be related to avoiding detection. Each upload provides an opportunity to further obfuscate or modify the malware to bypass existing detection signatures.

**Detailed Description of Techniques:**

*   **Clean Dropper:** The group wants a dropper that is not flagged by antivirus or EDR. This could involve techniques like:
    *   Polymorphism (changing the code structure while maintaining functionality).
    *   Metamorphism (rewriting the code completely each time).
    *   Code obfuscation (making the code harder to understand and analyze).
    *   Using a trusted binary to download and execute the malicious payload (Living off the Land).
*   **Encryption:** Encrypting the payload prevents static analysis by antivirus engines. Once the dropper is executed, it decrypts the payload in memory, potentially bypassing signature-based detection.
*   **Process Injection:** Injecting shellcode into a legitimate process (often a system process) is a common evasion technique. This allows the malicious code to run with the privileges of the target process and makes it harder to detect. The injection can be done by manipulating the memory of the target process. The shellcode itself could perform further malicious actions, such as downloading and executing additional payloads.
*   **Re-uploading HVNC:** Regularly re-uploading HVNC (Hidden Virtual Network Computing) could be related to avoiding detection. Each upload provides an opportunity to further obfuscate or modify the malware to bypass existing detection signatures.

```

**Summary of EDR/AV Evasion Techniques Discussed:**

The group is actively discussing methods to evade detection by security products, specifically EDR and antivirus solutions. Here's a breakdown of the techniques they are exploring:

1.  **File Extension Obfuscation (SCT, WSC):** They discuss using SCT (Windows Script Component) and WSC (Windows Script Component) files as a means of initial execution. They realize SCT/WSC might not work on x64 systems and are brainstorming ways to execute it, including embedding within PDFs. The goal is to leverage these file types to bypass initial security checks.

2.  **LNK File Exploitation:** They contemplate using LNK (shortcut) files to execute malicious payloads. They are aware that LNK files are often flagged, so they're looking for ways to make their LNK files less suspicious. This includes the following:
    *   **Overlaying Payloads:** Overlays can be hidden data within the LNK file to avoid initial detection.
    *   **PDF Embedding:** Embedding a LNK file within a PDF document.
    *   **Avoiding Downloads:** Aiming to avoid direct downloads associated with LNKs which are often flagged by security systems.

3.  **Mark-of-the-Web (MOTW) Bypass:**  MOTW is a flag added by Windows to files downloaded from the internet, indicating they should be treated with extra caution. They are aware that MOTW presence increases the likelihood of detection.

4.  **PDF Exploitation:** The group explores several methods related to PDFs:
    *   **Embedding LNK/HTML within PDFs:** Attempts to embed malicious LNK files or HTML code (containing LNKs) inside PDFs.  However, there's concern that JavaScript within the PDF will be easily detected.
    *   **Social Engineering with PDF + Root Certificate:** They consider creating a fake PDF, using social engineering to trick users into installing a "root certificate" to view the document, potentially a disguised malicious payload.
    *    **PDF as a Delivery Mechanism**: Create a Verisign themed PDF with instructions to install a "root certificate" to open the document.
    *   **PDF Landing Pages:**  Use PDFs that function as landing pages, containing links to malicious downloads. They discuss the need to rotate these landing pages frequently.

5.  **Regsvr32 with SCT:** Using regsvr32.exe (a legitimate Windows utility) to execute SCT files. The goal is to execute arbitrary code using a signed, trusted Windows binary.

6.  **DLL Side-Loading:** The idea of taking a legitimate application, reversing it, and injecting their malicious DLL, therefore making it look as a legitimate application.

7.  **Download/CURL Avoidance:** They recognize that EDR and AV are actively monitoring network traffic for suspicious downloads, particularly those initiated by `curl`. They discuss ways to avoid making external downloads, preferring to embed the entire payload within the initial dropper.

8.  **Payload Packing/Obfuscation:** Discuss the importance of having a good "crypter" and of general dll cryption, where they exchange DLLs to send through a crypter.

9.  **Process Injection:**  They refer to having a SOX5 bot that injects into processes

**Tools and Techniques:**

*   **SCT (Windows Script Component)**
*   **WSC (Windows Script Component)**
*   **LNK (Shortcut files)**
*   **Regsvr32:**  Windows utility for registering DLLs and SCT components.
*   **Certutil:** Attempting (and failing) to use certutil for payload execution.
*   **Powershell:** Attempting to use Powershell for payload execution, but deciding against it due to it being blocked on many networks.
*   **CMd:** Attempting to use CMD for payload execution.
*   **DLL Side Loading:** Method to execute dll through legitimate applications.
*   **Crypters:** Used to evade detection by antivirus software.
*   **Overlaying Payloads** Using Overlays in LNK files to conceal the actuall payload in LNK files.

**Targeting/Delivery Methods:**

*   **Email Attachments:** They prefer sending payloads as email attachments rather than relying on download links, as many networks now block external downloads.
*   **Spear Phishing:** Actively trying to infiltrate email accounts to reply into email threads.
*   **Exchange Access:** Gaining access to Exchange servers to send malicious attachments from compromised accounts.
*   **MSI Installer Delivery:** Delivering payloads through MSI installers.
*   **Legitimate Software Bundling:** Embedding payloads with legitimate software.

**Overall Strategy:**

The group's strategy revolves around using a combination of file-based and process-based techniques to bypass security controls. They are focused on social engineering, exploiting legitimate Windows utilities, and creating payloads that are difficult to detect through static analysis. They prioritize embedding the entire malicious chain within the initial payload to avoid detection of network activity.

*   **MSI Payload Detection and Bypassing:** Discusses the detection of malicious MSI (Microsoft Installer) files by antivirus software ("деф обновился", "палит уже вчерашний msi"). It also covers attempts to create "clean" MSIs and bypass AV detection.
*   **VBScript Payload Detection and Bypassing:** Similar to the above, focusing on VBScript files and how they're detected by antivirus software (referred to as "вакатак при скачивании"). Attempts are made to sign the VBScript with a certificate to bypass detection, with limited success. They also note the size of the MSI files being similar suggesting same stub being used. They try modifying hashes of the files.
*   **EDR Evasion/Defense Evasion:** Discussion around turning off "деф" (Defender) and using a Powershell script ("dDef.ps1") to "completely cut it off under the root". They mention that the script itself might be detected, and offer recommendations to disable real-time protection and cloud delivery features before running it. Discussion of what the script does "cut it off under the root","деткитть","в MsMpEng.exe появится". 
*   **Rubeus**: Usage of `execute-assembly Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:DC01$ /certificate:MII.. /ptt` a tool for Kerberos abuse, is shown as post-exploitation tool in the Cobalt Strike beacon.
* **Credential Dumping**: `POSTDump.exe` which is used to get registry keys and "protected process handle", then unloaded the driver, deleting the reg key and driver from disk. Then they "restore it run: python3 dump-restore.py " so they can open the dumped LSASS.

It's important to note the use of terms like "палит" (paliт) meaning "detects" and "дефф" (deff) referring to Windows Defender which signifies active efforts to avoid detection by security software.

*   **[2023-10-11 07:52:29] @lapa:** "...вообще-то это подписанный вбс" - This indicates the use of code signing to bypass security measures, including antivirus and potentially EDR. Signed scripts are often trusted more readily by security software.

*   **[2023-10-11 07:54:35] @lapa:** "...чтобы хеши были разные" - Reinforces the idea of changing the hash to avoid detection.
*   **[2023-10-11 08:45:39] @lapa:** "...попробую без серта" - Testing if detection is tied to the certificate itself, which would imply the cert has been blacklisted by AV/EDR vendors.

Here's the content from the chat log that discusses EDR evasion techniques, antivirus bypasses, and EDR killer tools or methods:

**1. Crypting and Code Obfuscation (Antivirus Bypass):**

*   Several participants mention "крипт" (crypt), short for "криптовать" (to encrypt/crypt), and "криптануть" which involves obfuscating or encrypting the payload (DLL, loader, JS) to evade antivirus detection.
*   **Burito** and **Ben** are identified as "криптеры" (crypters), suggesting they specialize in obfuscating code to bypass antivirus software.  The user @usernamegg notes that "Бурито тоже хороший криптер" (Burito is also a good crypter).
*   They use different methods, including "инжектирование в другой длл" (injecting into another DLL) which likely refers to DLL injection or similar techniques to hide malicious code within legitimate processes.  The user @usernamegg says that, "бен еще не пришел который делает инжектирование в другой длл" (Ben hasn't arrived yet who does injection into another dll).
* User @usernamess suggests that "ев сертами подписывай файлы после крипта тогда" (sign files with EV certificates after crypting). This would give the files a legitimate appearance, and help bypass security products.
* Another user explains that "файлы делаю макс легальные, беру проекты с гитхаба и туда пихаю код наш" which means "I make the files as legal as possible, I take projects from github and put our code there". This technique attempts to evade AV detection by masquerading as legitimate software.
* The chat discusses techniques like комперсия "RtlCompressBuffer" and "разбивать энтропию" to reduce file size and possibly evade AV detection.
*  User @w notes in reference to anti-virus bypass, "завтар под trend micro сделаю, а то norton и symantec уже помойка палит" (I will make it for Trend Micro tomorrow, because Norton and Symantec are already garbage). This suggests the group is actively targeting specific antivirus products for evasion.

**2. Code Signing and Certificate Manipulation:**

*   The group heavily relies on code signing certificates (especially EV certificates) to give their malicious files a veneer of legitimacy.
*   They're concerned about "окошко" (window/popup) that appears even when the file is signed, indicating that Windows doesn't fully trust the certificate.  They discuss the importance of using "крутой серт" (a good certificate).
*   There's a problem with using a signed JS, with user @lapa reporting it looks "какбудто и не подписаны" (as if it wasn't signed).
*   User @lapa says "просто еще вопрос а как именно подписать, не написано" (just another question, but how exactly to sign, it is not written). This shows the user may be unfamiliar with some details of signing and manipulating files.
*  The group considers different locations for signing files.

**3. Droppers and Loaders:**

*   The use of droppers and loaders is implied, as they are distributing DLLs and executables.  A "лоадер криптануть для масс просыла" (loader to encrypt for mass mailing) is explicitly mentioned.
*  The threat actors also utilize stagers and stageless payloads. Stagers allow for smaller initial payloads, while stageless payloads include the full malicious payload within the initial file.

**4. Process Injection and Hollowing:**

* Mention of making it "инжектирование в другой длл" (injecting into another DLL).
* The user @w asks if "process hollow Тоже не идет?", suggesting that process hollowing is one of the methods being used to mask the malicious code.
* A user suggests that for process hollowing "process hollow лучше stager бить, ибо там буфер небольшой" (It is better to use stager for process hollow, because the buffer is small there). Implying this is a technique they are aware of.

**5. Detection and Evasion Testing:**

*   They use "avcheck.net" and "АВ лабах" (AV labs) which is likely VirusTotal or similar services to check the detection rate of their payloads.  For example: "я проверил по авчеку чисто" (I checked according to AVcheck, it's clean).
* User @usernamegg says "я проверил по авчеку чисто" (I checked avcheck, it's clean), "W проверяет" (W is checking). Showing they use the service for AV testing.
*   The group considers the age of AV signatures.  It is also noted that "а в анубисе" it lies inside Anubis, which may refer to analyzing data within a sandbox environment.
*   The actors note a need to run a file, 'чтобы при запуске окошко всплывало' which means "to see if a popup will appear at startup". They want to check for user-level alerts being triggered.
* User @lapa mentions that it wants to test whether 'в подписаном файле видно где подписан серт и майкрософт палит это' which translated means 'in the signed file it is visible where the cert is signed and microsoft detects it'.
* A user notes, 'ладно будем выдавать тебе доступ по рдп к тачке где стоит ав будешь с дебагером пробовать слепить крипт, так будет эффективней , ну так же как на бентли с тобой сделали и мы по сути обошли нормально софос и у нас был фаил с которым мы по сети двигались как дома без как их либо проблем' which means 'Okay, we will give you access via rdp to the machine where the AV is installed, you will try to create a crypt with the debugger, this will be more effective, just like we did with you on the Bentley and we essentially bypassed Sophos normally and we had a file with which we moved around the network like at home without any problems'.

**6. Certificate Revocation:**

**7. Def and Adware:**

* User @w notes that it uses 'новый стаб от fortinet' which roughly translates to 'new stub from fortinet'. However, it notes that it is being 'палит все таки' by 'деф'. 
* User notes 'но adware его палит' which translated means, 'but adware exposes it'.

*   The user @w mentions "вот эта сетка реверсер была помечена, перенес ее в виктим" (this network was marked as a reverser, moved it to victim).

**9. Discussion of Security Product Effectiveness and Targeting:**

*   The group discusses the effectiveness of various antivirus products and how to target specific defenses.
*  The user @usernamess is on a network protected by 'деф, софос, сплунк, кортекс' meaning that it has access to the 'DEF, Sophos, Splunk, Cortex'
* Also the use of AMSI is described to "обойти деф" or bypass def.

[2023-11-01]
----------
Here's the content related to EDR evasion, antivirus bypasses, and EDR killer tools from the chat log, along with detailed descriptions:

**1. EDR Killer Tools/Methods and Techniques:**

*   **ESXi VM Killing:** The primary focus is on killing ESXi virtual machines as part of the ransomware process. This is achieved using a tool named `encryptor` with the `-killesxi` flag. The command `nohup sh -c '/tmp/encryptor -killesxi' > /dev/null 2>&1 &` is repeatedly used to execute this killer functionality in the background.
*   **Process Whitelisting/Killing:** A script is shown that attempts to kill ESXi processes, but with a whitelisting mechanism to avoid killing legitimate system processes. The script reads a list of whitelisted process names from `/tmp/list.txt` (which is noted as not found in the logs). It then iterates through running VM processes (obtained using `esxcli vm process list`), compares the process names to the whitelist, and kills any process that *doesn't* match the whitelist. The `kill -9` command is used for forceful termination. This can be considered an EDR "disabling" or impairment technique, even though the script targets VM processes rather than EDR specifically.

    ```bash
    export whitelist=$(cat /tmp/list.txt); export AWKRESULT="$(esxcli vm process list | grep -E 'Cartel|Display' | awk -F":" '{print $2}')"; export totalPassed=0; echo "$AWKRESULT" | while read -r line; do totalPassed=$((totalPassed+1)); remainder=$(($totalPassed%2)); if [ $remainder -eq 1 ]; then esxiname=$(echo "$AWKRESULT" | awk -v var="$(($totalPassed+1))" 'NR==var {print $0}'); echo "$whitelist" | while read -r whitelist_item; do if [ -z "${whitelist_item}" ]; then kill -9 $line; echo "kill $line"; continue; fi; if [ "$( echo "$esxiname" | grep -ci "$whitelist_item" )" -ge 1 ]; then echo "$esxiname in whitelist"; else kill -9 $line; fi; done; echo "$totalPassed: $line esxi name: $esxiname"; fi; done;
    ```

**2. EDR Evasion Techniques/Antivirus Bypass:**

*   **File Signature Evasion:** There are frequent discussions on whether payloads (JS, DLL, ISO) are being detected by antivirus. They discuss "paling" and "deff", likely referring to detection by AV/EDR solutions. The group actively tries to create "clean" payloads that are not immediately detected. They talk about "обфускация"(obfuscation), attempting to make their code less obvious to signature-based detection.
*   **Certificate-Based Bypass:** The group uses code signing certificates to sign their payloads (DLLs, JS). They are concerned about the certificates being revoked ("заблочили", "сняли серт") and are constantly seeking new and valid certificates. They are also facing issues with code signing. There are mentions "ошибка на подпись", indicating that the certificate signing process failed, possibly due to various reasons including problems with the certificate itself or access to the certificate store. They specifically mention the need for EV (Extended Validation) certificates, likely to bypass reputation-based detections. They discussed command line usage for code signing, but also struggle with using new EV certs, with mentions of a need to scan a hash first.
*   **Process Injection/Hollowing:**  One user mentions "запустил из под бота, инжекты были успешные, но сам кейлогер не запустился" and suggests to "сгрузите его через process hollow" or "поменяйте процесс". This means that they were using some legitimate processes to load the malware to avoid being detected by security solution.
*   **AV/EDR Testing:** Before deploying payloads, they are testing them against AV/EDR solutions to determine if they are detected. They use services like "avcheck.net" and check against "вакатак".
*   **HTML Smuggling and Payload Obfuscation:** There's a reference to a technique involving sending ".html" files, but acknowledging that these are now often blocked. They also discuss sending .html files that "blob" the malicious content. There are further discussions about packing payloads into .ISO images. Several payloads were tested to check bypass capability: JS, .DLLs, EXEs
*   **File extension changes, compression and different file format embedding:** The group also tries some file format changing to bypass AV detections. Compressing the payload inside .ZIP archives to avoid initial scans.  Discussions on HTML containing ISO or JS containing .DLL
*   **VBA Macro use:**  ""когда слали .html файл"  "блобом выдавали" - They were injecting blob of code inside HTML file using javascript to execute malicious VBA scripts.
*   **MOTW (Mark of the Web) bypass:** Mentions of dlls that when downloaded through browsers are flagged as "Mark of The Web" and this is how it triggers Anti-Virus on system. One user suggests to download these files through "curl" to avoid this.

**3. General Reconnaissance and Tools:**

*   **ESXi Commands:** They use `esxcli vm process list` to list running virtual machines, `esxcli system settings encryption set` and `esxcli system settings encryption get` to manage encryption settings.  `vim-cmd` commands are used to control VMs, including listing VMs (`vim-cmd vmsvc/getallvms`) and powering them on (`vim-cmd vmsvc/power.on <vmid>`).
*   **File Searching:** The `find` command combined with `tail` and `grep` is used to search for specific "magic" strings within files, presumably to identify encrypted or infected files. This implies that the ransomware uses a specific file marker or signature. For example: `find / -name "*" -type f -exec sh -c "tail -c 13 {} |  grep -i '1wvg7bxcfoa'" \;`

**4. Defense Evasion Discussion:**

* Some tactics of the attacker to "minimise footprint" like creating SOCKS proxy.
* They discuss using Cobalt Strike ("Кобу") despite it being illegal in their region (Russia).
* User suggests to use legitimate tools already installed in the victim system (e.g. signed Python binary).

The overarching theme is a cat-and-mouse game with security solutions, where the attackers are constantly adapting their techniques to evade detection. The group prioritizes getting a "clean" initial payload on the system, and a big part of the conversation revolves around payload encoding, obfuscation, using certificates, and quickly reacting to detection signatures. The logs illustrate that the evasion techniques aren't always reliable, requiring frequent rebuilds and tests.

[2023-11-04]

Here's the analysis of the chat log, focusing on EDR evasion, antivirus bypasses, and EDR killer techniques:

*   **".hta and .chm preparation and signing"**: User `w` mentions they have `.hta` and `.chm` files ready and plan to sign them to see what happens. This relates to bypassing security measures, as signed files are often trusted more readily by operating systems and security software. Attackers often use these file types to deliver malicious payloads because they can execute arbitrary code.

*   **"MSI building"**: User `w` says they will work with `.msi` files tomorrow and add infrastructure. `.msi` files are Windows Installer packages, another common method for deploying software. Creating malicious `.msi` installers is a well-known attack vector.

*   **"WinRAR exploit testing"**: User `w` suggests looking at a WinRAR exploit and potentially sending it out on Monday to "see what happens." User `usernamegg` confirms they will test the WinRAR exploit the next day. Exploiting vulnerabilities in popular software like WinRAR is a common tactic to bypass security measures.

*   **"Checking infrastructure and system operability"**: User `w` mentions checking infrastructure and system operability, which could involve deploying and testing the effectiveness of different tools and payloads against target systems.

*   **"Mirror testing"**: User `usernameyy` mentions using a mirror to see what will happen, implying testing of payloads or methods in a controlled environment to avoid immediate detection.

*   **"Driver-based persistence mechanism"**: User `w` reviews a video link shared by `usernamegg` and refers to the discussed method as a "ironclad foothold, with unsigned drivers that hook directly into the boot loader," indicating a sophisticated, low-level persistence mechanism that would likely be difficult for EDR/AV to detect. The shared link: `https://streamable.com/nwydc5`

**Detailed Description of Techniques and Methods Discussed:**

1.  **Signed HTA/CHM files**: The discussion indicates a plan to use `.hta` (HTML Application) and `.chm` (Compiled HTML Help) files. These file types can execute code and are often used in attacks. Signing the files could help bypass initial AV/EDR detection by making them appear legitimate.

2.  **Malicious MSI packages**: Creating malicious Windows Installer (`.msi`) packages to deliver payloads is a common technique. These packages can be used to install malware or execute commands on a system.

3.  **WinRAR exploit**: The mention of a WinRAR exploit suggests the group is exploring exploiting a known or potentially zero-day vulnerability in WinRAR to execute code or gain access to systems.

4.  **Driver-based persistence in the bootloader**: The group describes a method involving unsigned drivers placed directly in the bootloader. This provides a very low-level and persistent foothold that can be extremely difficult for security software to detect and remove.

5.  **Mirror Testing**: The use of a "mirror" is likely a reference to a testing environment that mimics the target's environment without directly affecting the target. This allows the attackers to test their payloads and methods without alerting the target's security systems.

[2023-11-05]
----------
The chat log contains discussions related to AV bypass techniques, specifically:

*   **File Extension Manipulation:** The group discusses bypassing security measures by renaming an HTA (HTML Application) file to a JS (JavaScript) file, signing it with a certificate, and then renaming it back to HTA. This is to circumvent the restriction of signing HTA files directly. This technique aims to trick the system into trusting the file due to the valid signature applied when it was a JS file, potentially bypassing AV/EDR checks that specifically target unsigned or untrusted HTA files.

*   **Exploiting WinRAR Vulnerability:** The group is actively testing a WinRAR exploit (likely CVE-2023-38831). They download potentially malicious RAR files (vzrJ.rar, tcO.rar, rFTK.rar, aIqT.rar, WSClH.rar, UbWn.rar, QjxU.rar, PMSu.rar, LNZE.rar, HUqSs.rar) and use a publicly available vulnerable version of WinRAR. The goal is to deliver malicious payloads by exploiting the CVE-2023-38831.

*   **Bypassing Signature Restrictions on HTA files** The group has a discussion about not being able to sign hta files directly with their existing certificate.

*   **General AV/EDR Testing:** The group is actively testing various file types (.hta, .chm, .bat) against the "деф" (likely referring to a security solution or antivirus software) to determine if they are detected.

This is a common EDR evasion technique.
*   **Port modification:** The group discusses creating "artifact\_x86\_port23.dll" and "beacon\_x86\_port23.dll," which are identical to existing payloads but run on a different port. This likely aims to bypass network-based detection rules or firewalls that might be blocking the original payload's communication. Changing the port is a simple evasion tactic.

**Detailed Description of Techniques, Tools, and Methods:**

1.  **Dynamic Analysis Evasion:** The group is actively struggling with dynamic analysis detection. They are trying to "clean" their malware to evade this type of detection. This includes:

    *   **Changing the Process:** Altering the process name/identifier associated with the malicious activity in order to prevent identification.
    *   **Changing Resources:** Modifying the resources used by the malware to avoid signature-based detection.
    *   **Recompiling:** Rebuilding the DLL with slight alterations to change its signature.
    *   **Code Morphing:** Developing new algorithms for morphing the code to evade detection, implying polymorphism or metamorphism.
    *   **WinAPI Calls:** Using WinAPI calls and avoiding other methods that have been detected.
    *   **Garbage Code:** Adding useless or misleading code to confuse analysis.
2.  **Static Analysis Evasion:** There is one mention of static analysis detection.
    *   **Cleaning Build:** Removing static signatures from the malware that trigger detection.
3.  **`regsvr32` Abuse:** The group is using the `regsvr32` utility to execute DLLs. This is a common technique to bypass initial defenses because `regsvr32` is a legitimate Windows tool.
    *   `regsvr32 /s file.dll`
    *   `regsvr32 /s /i file.dll`
4.  **AMSI Bypass:** There is a failed attempt at bypassing AMSI (Anti-Malware Scan Interface) through encrypted PowerShell. This shows an awareness of AMSI as a defensive measure.
5.  **In-Memory Execution:** There is a mention of running Cobalt Strike beacon in memory, indicating awareness of anti-forensic techniques.
6.  **Service Whitelisting/Exclusion:** The group is creating a whitelist of services *not* to terminate, specifically listing services related to virtualization and Hyper-V to avoid crashing systems and raising suspicions.
7.  **DLL Recompilation:** Discussion of recompiling DLLs in attempt to evade detection by AV solutions.
8.  **DLL "Cleaning":** Discussion of "cleaning" DLL files.
9.  **RunPE:** Suggestion on injecting payloads.

[2023-11-24]
----------
Here's an analysis of the chat log, focusing on EDR evasion techniques, antivirus bypasses, and EDR killer tools or methods:

*   **DLL Side-Loading:** User 'w' describes DLL side-loading as a persistence technique. The discussion mentions QBot utilizing this method. The core idea is to place a malicious DLL in a directory where a legitimate application will load it instead of its intended DLL. This can bypass AV since the initial application process is trusted. "QBot used the technique DLL Side-Loading"

*   **Process Injection/Hooking:** User 'w' discusses the challenges of process injection and hooking due to increased security measures. The chat highlights that these techniques are more frequently monitored and protected against by modern security solutions. "я заебался пиздец, они навесили почти на на каждую функцию"

*   **Code Obfuscation/Encryption:** User 'w' suggests morphing code and encrypting it to avoid signature-based AV detection. The idea is to make the malicious code's fingerprint unrecognizable to traditional AV engines that rely on known malware signatures. "код можно поморфить который не будет палится никакими АВ в мире даже по сигнатуре потому что можно сделать его шифрованным"

*   **"Cleaning" bots:** There is a mention of "чистишь бота" which most likely mean removing any traces that the bot was there, which could mean wiping logs.

*   **"криптовать и проверить":** Crypting (encrypting) is mentioned as a method to bypass AV/EDR. The term "закриптовать" (to encrypt) and "криптовать" is used numerous times.

*   **Certs for bypassing AV/EDR:** There is discussion about EV certificates used for signing the files and this will bypass some prompts and alerts during installation.

*   **"а сборку его оттестировать":** The actors are discussing about testing their payloads.

*   **"отрубить отправку семплов":** The actors are discussing about disabling any sample submissions to vendors when testing.

*   **"подпи ши ее с листе что она с NIK LDR":** It means sign the executable by NIK LDR.

*   **Cobalt Strike Aggressor Scripts:** Mention of Cobalt Strike and Aggressor scripts as means to implement and test encryption methods. This may be a custom encryption module to evade detection.

*   **UDRL usage in artifact kit:** Usage of UDRL in artifact kit suggests it is a method to bypass detections during creation of artifacts.

**Description of techniques, tools, or methods discussed:**

*   **MSIX with EV Certificate:** The group discusses the use of MSIX installers signed with Extended Validation (EV) certificates to bypass security measures. EV certificates are more trusted and can help the MSIX installer to be seen as clean.
*   **Private Dropper/Loader JS Evasion:**  The group mentions that one member, baragozer, had initially struggled with the "DEf" (likely referring to Windows Defender) detecting their dropper/loader. They reworked it after studying the EDR (likely Windows Defender), after which the EDR was bypassed.
*   **Crypted DLLs:** A member mentions that a crypted DLL worked, implying the use of encryption to bypass security detections. This suggests a simple method of AV/EDR evasion by encrypting the payload.
*   **AV Killer Tools:**  There is a brief mention of purchasing "AV killer tools." This directly references the use of tools specifically designed to disable or bypass antivirus software.
*   **Silently triggering Lockers:** Group members report their lockers being signatured and failing to execute after being signatured by Windows Defender. They also mention re-encrypting them.
*   **LSASS Dump Exploit:** Acquisition of LSASS dump exploits - used to dump credentials and sensitive data from LSASS process, which is often protected by security products. This could be used as a post-exploitation step.

**Detailed description of the techniques, tools, or methods discussed:**

*   **"Крипт" (Kript) - Crypter:** Refers to a tool used to obfuscate or encrypt malicious files (e.g., EXEs, DLLs) to evade detection by antivirus and EDR solutions. It's implied that these crypters change the file's signature or appearance, so it doesn't match known malware signatures.
*   **"Дефенжер" (Defender) - Windows Defender Bypass:** Discussions involve finding ways to bypass Windows Defender, a built-in antivirus solution.
*   **"Перекриптовать" - Re-encrypt:** If a file or payload is being detected, the group plans to re-encrypt it using a different or updated crypter to avoid detection. This shows a constant effort to stay ahead of security signature updates.
*   **"Подписать" - Signing:** This probably refers to code signing, the process of digitally signing a file to verify its authenticity and integrity. It's used to bypass security measures that trust signed files.
*   **"Виндоустейджер" (Window Stager):** The use of varied payloads (EXE, DLL, etc.) and Windows stagers to attempt to bypass AV/EDR.
*  **"Обфускации, хеширование API, кодировку для каждой используемой строки, а также множественные проверки для предотвращения запуска в песочнице."** complex techniques of hiding payload

The chat also revealed several mentions of specific software and practices that are directly related to EDR/AV evasion:
*   **Cobalt Strike stageless payload:** The use of Cobalt Strike payloads is mentioned which are often used to establish a foothold on a system. This is also related to custom stagers for delivery.
*   **API Hooking:** This relates to manipulating the Windows API calls to hide malicious activity or change the behavior of legitimate software.
* "Threathlockerservice / threatlockertray" potentially related to EDR service to try and detect.

Here's the EDR/AV-related content extracted from the chat log:

*   **DLL Loading and Execution:** A significant portion of the chat focuses on using `rundll32.exe` to execute DLLs. This technique is frequently employed for bypassing security measures, as `rundll32.exe` is a legitimate Windows process and its use can blend in with normal system activity. There are several mentions of different DLL files and commands to execute them:

    *   `rundll32.exe C:\Windows\TRANSBUSINT_c.dll,CloseNow`
    *   `rundll32.exe \\ADL-CITSESS-90\C$\Windows\TRANSBUSINT_c.dll,CloseNow START FROM SHARES`
    *   `rundll32.exe dll.dll,CloseNow`
*   **Whitelisting and Process Injection:**  One user mentions a "whitelist of processes" into which code should be injected: `@usernameyy: ...я давал вайтлист процессов, в эти процессы инжектите`. The whitelist includes a broad range of common Windows processes:
    *   `sihost.exe`, `cmd.exe`, `lsass.exe`, `rundll32.exe`, `regsvr32.exe`, `powershell.exe`, `svchost.exe`, `conhost.exe`, `explorer.exe`, `dllhost.exe`, `msvsmon.exe`, `vctip.exe`, `winlogon.exe`, `devenv.exe`, `servicehub.host.clr.x64.exe`, `servicehub.host.clr.x86.exe`, `servicehub.host.clr.exe`, `servicehub.identityhost.exe`, `servicehub.testwindowstorehost.exe`, `servicehub.datawarehouseHost.exe`, `servicehub.vsdetouredhost.exe`, `vsdebugconsole.exe`, `msbuild.exe`, `fontdrvhost.exe`, `wudfhost.exe`, `dwm.exe`, `vboxservice.exe`, `dashost.exe`, `taskhostw.exe`, `runtimebroker.exe`, `vboxtray.exe`, `perfwatson2.exe`, `microsoft.servicehub.controller.exe`, `vsls-agent.exe`, `servicehub.settingshost.exe`, `servicehub.roslyncodeanalysisservice.exe`, `vcpkgsrv.exe`, `useroobebroker.exe`, `mspdbsrv.exe`, `servicehub.datawarehousehost.exe`, `standardcollector.service.exe`, `vssvc.exe`, `wmiprvse.exe`, `webviewhost.exe`, `msedgewebview2.exe`.

    This suggests a technique of injecting malicious code into legitimate processes to evade detection.
*   **EDR and AV Evasion:** There is frequent discussion around "крипт" (crypt), which likely refers to a crypter used to obfuscate malicious code and bypass antivirus or EDR solutions. They discuss different "крипты" from different developers:
    *   It is mentioned that a "крипт" from `muaddib6` did not bypass Trend Micro.
    *   The group uses and prefers a "крипт" created by someone named `burito`. It's considered superior for encrypting their locker.
    *   There's discussion about finding a "чистый" (clean) build, meaning one that isn't easily detected by security software. This emphasizes their awareness of AV/EDR systems.
*  **CrowdStrike Falcon:** They have difficulties working on networks with CrowdStrike Falcon installed. Falcon is blocking FTP access and actively preventing code execution.
*   **Trend Micro:** It is explicitly stated, "скрипт который сделал muaddib не обошел тредмикро" which translates to, "the script that muaddib made did not bypass Trend Micro" indicating they are actively trying to bypass this AV. Further, "тренмикро даже съедал локер". Also, "скрипт который сделал muaddib не обошел тредмикро6" which may indicate an older version of the AV (TrendMicro 6).
*   **Sophos:** "nu Sophos zhestki" which translates to "Sophos is tough" indicating that they have difficulties bypassing this AV.
*   **SentinelOne:** Mentioned in context of target network: `TRANSBUSINT | group targets | 861srv/1 trust | Hyper-V | $710M | 9 Central Central Park Blvd, Larbert, Stirlingshire, FK5 4RU, United Kingdom | www.alexander-dennis.com | TrendMicro/Splunk/Sentinel/Sophos | DW | NAME LOCKER | DATE LOCK |`
*  **Windows Defender:** Discussing `hotelplan.co.uk`, a user states "там windows defender zloy stoit", which translates to, "there is an evil Windows Defender installed."
*   **EDR Killing:** The script `CloseNow`, in both DLL and EXE form, is mentioned. This suggests a potential EDR-killing component, likely intended to terminate security processes.
*   **Shellcode Obfuscation:** They mention having to bypass "деф" (def), which is short for defender and likely means Windows Defender, suggesting they need to obfuscate their shellcode.
*   **Vulnerability Exploitation:** Mention of CVE-2023-36394, used to add a new user to the Administrators group, possibly circumventing some access controls or detection mechanisms.

    *   The group discusses using "search.exe" (likely a renamed and modified binary) with the `-c` flag to execute commands. They acknowledge that it doesn't work on updated systems (KB5032304, KB5032198).

```
- EDR (Endpoint Detection and Response) evasion techniques
    * The group discusses the presence of Sophos EDR on the target network and the difficulty in bypassing it.
    * "Везде софос накатили" (Sophos is deployed everywhere) indicates a widespread EDR presence.
    * "мы даже по шаре не можем запустить" (we can't even run it from the share) suggests Sophos is blocking execution of their tools.
    * "было вчера 4 тачки без софоса. сегодня все с софосом" (yesterday there were 4 machines without Sophos. today they all have Sophos) indicates the target is actively deploying EDR in response to the attack.
    *  One member mentions taking steps to disable "def" and calling in a "kobu" (Cobalt Strike likely) to then have Sophos deployed on all machines.
    * They discuss using cryptors (software that encrypts executables to evade AV detection) and specifically mention "криптованный не обходит ничего" (the encrypted one doesn't bypass anything).
    * They plan to have a cryptor ("burito") create a custom cryptor specifically for Sophos:  "надо крипт сделать локера для софоса" (need to make a crypt for the locker for Sophos) and "он там с дебагером сделает крипт локера для софоса" (he will make a crypt for locker for Sophos there with a debugger).
    * The team is looking to manually create a "locker" to bypass Sophos' process execution prevention:  "локер реально закриптовать что бы обойти софос потому что это наш собственный софт а кобу почти нереально" (it is really to encrypt locker that would bypass Sophos because this is our own software, but cobaltstrike is almost impossible)
    * They intend to open an RDP session on a machine with Sophos to allow "burito" to create a bypass: "откроем тачку по рдп где стоит софос дадим креды от это впски бурито он там с дебагером сделает крипт локера для софоса" (let's open a machine via RDP that has sophos installed and will give creds from the vps to burito and he will create a crypt locker for sophos there via debugger)
    * "там процесс ехекюте режет и не запускается" (the process execute cuts it and does not start) illustrates Sophos' process execution prevention capabilities.
    * "но полит нас в статике когда начинает лочить он просто рубит процесс" (but polit us in static when it starts to lock, it just kills the process) states that when the file is about to lock something, it will kill the process. "Полит" is likely short for "политИка", policy.

- Antivirus bypasses
    *The conversation includes attempts to run their tools from a network share, which are being blocked by Sophos.
    *They mention having a crypter, but it is not bypassing the AV.

Here's the analysis of the chat log focusing on EDR evasion, antivirus bypasses, and EDR killer tools/methods:

**EDR Evasion Techniques / Antivirus Bypasses:**

*   **Process Injection:** There is significant discussion surrounding process injection as an evasion technique. The actors discuss the potential for detection, and relative stealth when injecting into the parent process vs. a third-party process.

    *   @usernamenn notes at [2023-12-10 12:20:08] and [2023-12-10 12:20:16] that "инжекты палятся в каких то случая больше чем просто запуск dll" (injections are detected more often in some cases than simply running a DLL). This suggests an awareness of injection techniques being a potential detection point.

    *   @usernamenn discusses injecting shellcode vs reflective DLL injection at [2023-12-10 12:19:29].

    *   @cameron777 claims their technique is "ne palevni" - not easily detected - at [2023-12-10 12:20:01].

    *   The group discusses injecting modules at [2023-12-10 12:20:36].

    *   @usernamenn states that injecting into your own parent process is less detectable than injecting into a third-party process ([2023-12-10 12:21:05] and [2023-12-10 12:21:12]).

*   **Thread Pool Injection:** At [2023-12-10 10:50:37] and [2023-12-10 10:50:49] a technique of process injection using windows thread pools is introduced.
    *   Links to the PoolParty tool by SafeBreach-Labs are provided, as well as a BlackHat presentation.

*   **"TranceQuantum.exe" Behavior:** This executable appears to use injection and self-deletion as evasion techniques:

    *   @cameron777 at [2023-12-10 14:30:17] shares the file "TranceQuantum.exe".
    *   At [2023-12-10 14:34:31], after @usernamenn runs tasklist, @cameron777 clarifies "nu tam zhe injekt" (well, there's injection).
    *   @usernamenn notes at [2023-12-10 14:36:15] that after the injection, the file self-deletes: "+ самоудаление файла который был запущен" (+ self-deletion of the file that was launched).

*   **Avast Antivirus:** At [2023-12-10 14:00:35] @cameron777 mentions "avast tam", and then at [2023-12-10 14:31:37] @usernamenn says "не запустился твой файл" (your file didn't run). @cameron777 responds with "da vozmozhno avastu chto to ne nravitsya" (yes, maybe Avast doesn't like something). This indicates the group is aware that Avast might be blocking their payloads.

**EDR Killer Tools/Methods:**

*   No explicit EDR killer tools are mentioned. However, process injection can be used to disable or tamper with EDR agents. The group's focus on process injection and bypassing security products implies a potential goal of subverting EDR and antivirus solutions.

**Detailed Description of Techniques, Tools, and Methods:**

*   **Discussion of AV and EDR blocking actions:** The log mentions that Avast is "glushing" processes. This refers to the AV blocking or interfering with the execution of malware or associated malicious processes.
*   **PoolParty Injection Technique:** A new injection technique is introduced using a presentation and Github link. This process injection is based on windows thread pools.
*   **DLL Crypting:** DLLs are being crypted, and the process of crypting DLLs to evade detection. Also it requests for auto-cryption from MrLapis.
*   **Quakbot cleaning:** User 'Chuck' mentions he has "cleaned" Quakbot, likely referring to efforts to remove or modify its code to evade detection.
*   **AMS I Bypass:**  Discussion regarding AMSI (Anti-Malware Scan Interface) bypass with Powershell to bypass windef which implies that they are familiar with techniques to disable or circumvent AMSI.
*   **EV Code Signing:** There is a reference to EV (Extended Validation) code signing, used for signing MSI installers to increase trust and bypass some security warnings. The group also discusses whether they are "heating" the certificate to remove alerts.
*   **AV Detection:** Discussion about Windows Defender starts to detect the MSI files.
*  **Anti-Analysis:** Discussion about how Anti-Virus can check JS file after 10 minutes.
*   **VM-Based Execution and Code Alteration:** Discussion on how to create VMs to bypass AMSI-based scanning, which are to be spun up every 3 hours. It also focuses on quickly checking newly crypted malware or re-signing for known-good certification.
*  **"Cleaning" Malware:** Discussion about whether they"cleaned" malware before they executed it.
*   **Dropper Usage:** Discussion of Dropper usage to bypass AV alerts.
*   **RunDLL32:** Discussion on how to use RUNDLL32 for process execution.
* **AV Solution Discussion:** Discussion on what the AV solution is (Sophos or ESET) at certain bot location and how they did not run Koba so it wouldn't get detected.&#x20;

The chat log contains a significant amount of discussion related to EDR evasion techniques and antivirus bypasses:

*   **General AV/EDR Evasion:** The group frequently discusses whether their payloads are detected by antivirus (AV) or other security products. They use terms like "чисто" (clean) to indicate that a file is not detected. The members also seem to have test systems with various AV solutions installed to evaluate their payloads.
*   **Dropper/Loader Obfuscation:** Discussion on wrapping payloads within scripts or loaders to avoid direct detection of the malicious binary.  One user mentions a script that "их льет в рандомные папки" (drops them in random folders).
*   **DLL Hiding/Injection:** There is a focus on using DLLs, and potentially injecting them into legitimate processes (as shown by the use of rundll32.exe and discussions of creating new processes).
*   **Certificate Manipulation:**  The group discusses using code signing certificates to sign their payloads, but they note that these certificates can be quickly flagged and become ineffective. They actively seek new certificates and monitor their detection rates.
*   **Domain Reputation:** They consider the "trustworthiness" of domains used for hosting payloads and redirecting traffic, implying they understand that reputation-based detection can affect their operations.
*   **Traffic Cloaking/Redirection:** They discuss techniques for cloaking traffic, such as using redirects to Dropbox and setting up subdomains to hide malicious activity.
*   **Proofpoint Evasion:** One user (@usernameugway) specifically mentions testing their email spoofing techniques against Proofpoint, a popular email security solution that includes real-time analysis of email content.  This suggests active efforts to bypass advanced email defenses.
*   **DNS Tunneling and SOCKS proxies through DNS:** A user discusses injecting through slow DNS socks, this is likely to bypass EDR on host by communicating though DNS channel.
*   **Fileless attacks/Living-off-the-Land:**  The discussion about Powershell and CMD suggest the attackers using default tools to change password , adding users etc.

There's no clear mention of specific "EDR killer" tools in the logs but instead it seems like they are actively trying to figure out methods to circumvent EDR.

**Detailed Description of Techniques, Tools, or Methods Discussed:**

*   **Bypassing SentinelOne:** There's an explicit goal to bypass SentinelOne, indicating it's a primary target for detection evasion.

*   **Crypting/Obfuscation Techniques:** The group discusses the freshness and effectiveness of their crypters (file encryptors). They acknowledge that even with a few detections, a crypted file can be useful for initial access. There are conversations about generating fresh crypts. They are using a product called redGuard that focuses on obfuscating the payload.

*   **Profile Manipulation and Customization (Cobalt Strike):**
    *   The group emphasizes the importance of proper Cobalt Strike profile configuration. There are discussions around "post-ex," "stage," and "process-inject" settings within the profile, suggesting attempts to modify how Cobalt Strike injects and executes code in memory.
    *   Discussion about specific configurations which will help them evade AV/EDR's. "уклонение со своими" and "подредачить"

*   **PE stomping:** The configuration options discussed suggest the usage of PE stomping as an evasion technique, with discussion on modules like netshell.dll being overwritten.

*   **ElusiveMice GitHub Repository:** A link to the ElusiveMice repository is shared. This repository contains offensive security tools and techniques, including those related to AV/EDR evasion.

*   **Dynamic Analysis and Runtime Detection Awareness:** They realize the issues they will run into with runtime detection and are working to avoid being detected in that manner. 

*   **Userwx and Process Injection:** Discussion of modifying permissions, to make code read/write/execute and RWX as the permissions. This is a known way to attempt to make memory regions used by code to be less suspicious to security products.

*   **DNS Beacon Usage:** The discussions include adjustments related to Domain Name System (DNS) beacons within Cobalt Strike, implying an attempt to use DNS for covert communication and control.

*   **Process Injection:** They mention VirtualAllocEx, NtMapViewOfSection, and other methods for process injection, showing awareness of different approaches.

*   **Anti-AMSI Measures:** Setting 'amsi_disable' to true is likely a direct attempt to disable the Anti-Malware Scan Interface (AMSI) in Windows, which security products use to scan scripts and code.

*   **Droppers and DLL Injection:** There's a proposition to rewrite components as droppers, suggesting that they intend to use DLL injection techniques to bypass security measures.

*   **Code Signing Server:** The group intends to set up a server for signing their malicious files.  Valid code signing certificates can be used to bypass reputation-based detection in AV/EDR products. It is implied that getting the code signed is key to making the attacks work and the members reference a desire to set up an exe and file signing system.

*   **Use of "RedGuard" to Hide C2 Infrastructure:** Discussion of using redGuard, this may be used for domain fronting.

**Detailed Description of Techniques, Tools, or Methods:**

*   **EDR Identification:** The group actively identifies EDR solutions present on target systems. Specifically, they repeatedly mention **SentinelOne Singularity XDR**, **TrendMicro**, and **Carbon Black** as being present. They also discuss **Webroot** and **BASTA 3.0** in relation to a particular target.

*   **Credential Theft/Kerberos Exploitation:** The group discusses techniques to steal Kerberos tickets ("kerbi snimu"). Cameron states, "pod S1 mogu," implying he has a method to perform this under SentinelOne. This aligns with post-exploitation techniques used to move laterally within a network, potentially gaining access to resources that can bypass endpoint security.

*   **Process Injection / Shellcode:** The conversation mentions writing shellcode for Cobalt Strike .bin files "по поводу переписывания на шелл коды или плюс не нужно, весь софт который есть написал уже изначально на то что нужно, менять не нужно". Also the discussion about "GOC" that usernamegg is going to rewrite into shellcode "шеллкод будет" and a "ахуеешь )". The user @usernamenn doesn't think that "шелл код тоже не нужен в goc" however @usernamegg replies "если будет чистый" suggesting that that technique would help them evade detection. They are considering writing shellcode loaders that are not signatured. It is apparent that Cobalt Strike beacon are being detected by endpoint protections and they are seeking evasion techniques.

*   **Antivirus/EDR Bypasses (General):** The actors constantly discuss "чистый файл" and needing "крипт" (encryption/obfuscation). "Lapa" mentions "деф еще обновился", implying that the effectiveness of their current bypasses is diminishing.

*   **Obfuscation/Encryption ("крипт"):** A central theme is the need for "крипт," which involves encrypting or obfuscating their payloads to avoid detection. They are continually evaluating the "чистота" of their files using in-house testing or tools from providers like "lapis". This highlights their awareness of signature-based detection.

*   **Defense Evasion Testing & Code Development:** They employ "mrlapis", a third party testing service, to test their payloads. Also they run their own "сканер" in order to check the file. They discuss different methodologies to evade detection based on "динамике" suggesting they are monitoring their code's dynamic actions to evade behavioral detection. "mrlapis" is using infinite loops "бесконечный цикл - детекта нет" and telling the coder "чтобы убрал детект" hinting a potential evasion using timed actions.

*   **RunDLL32 Use & Detection:** The group is aware that using `rundll32.exe` is being detected "Behavior:Win32/PikaBot.ZX". They have implemented different payloads that launch the dll, but it seems like the behavior is raising the "Behavior:Win32/PikaBot.ZX" flag

*   **PowerShell Obfuscation:** One user suggests using PowerShell for DLL loading and boasts the ability to obfuscate the PowerShell code to avoid detection: "предпочтительнее пс конечно, ведь я умею ее обфуцировать детекторов не будет и времени меньше потеряем." This leverages PowerShell's presence in Windows environments and the ability to make its scripts harder to analyze.

*   **DLL Loading via PowerShell/CMD:** They discuss loading DLLs via `cmd` or `powershell` (PS/CMD): "сделать загрузку чистой dll через пс\кмд предпочтительнее пс конечно, ведь я умею ее обфуцировать детекторов не будет и времени меньше потеряем". This technique aims to bypass traditional file-based scanning by loading malicious code directly into memory. They also mention use of signed DLLs to potentially bypass protections: "он подписал я ему скрипт сделал и. сервак на убунту сделал он теперь за 5сек подписывает".

*   **Memory Injection:** A technique is mentioned involving loading DLLs directly into memory "dll segodnya zdelaet" , "zapusk v pamyati" likely to bypass file-based scanning. They also discuss using a "loader" to launch the DLL into memory.

*  **Exploiting Trust:** A valid approach is shown when the actor Cameron says "tam Symantec, no mozho file dobavit' v isklyuchenia no ne adminski dostup ya tak zapustil svoy" meaning he found a bypass to the AV, without administrator privileges, allowing it to bypass it's security rules

*   **Exclusive plugin переписывать "переписывать eclusive плагин, посольку там вроде winapi используется для протекта памяти и аллока, а это очень плохо"** The group has identified a tool "eclusive" that their "переписывать" coder uses as not good. The coder is re-writing it to remove the "winapi используется для протекта памяти и аллока" which is detected by endpoint detections tools

*   **Evasive delivery through JS and signed files** the actors use javascript in order to deliver signed files, in that manner JS not triggering any flag.

*   **Loaders and crypters** A constant theme across all the chat is to load signed dll files using loaders and crypters. It can be determined that PikaBot is one of the tools they are trying to load.

*   **Defense in depth is failing** An assumption can be taken based on their statement saying that if one method fails, a failsafe has to be used "но, если у него чекер авторизовался, то пусть все равно сбрасывает" and that "все равно конечно херово, там у всех орать будет деф"

*   **Anti-Forensics:** There's a mention of clearing CRT from Cobalt Strike's "слипмаске кобальта crt есть, вырежу" which is important to cover their tracks and that the activity in memory is less likely to be captured.

The ongoing struggles with detection indicate that the group is constantly adapting their techniques to evade EDR and antivirus solutions. They are aware of the limitations of their methods and are actively seeking new ways to stay ahead of security measures. The focus on obfuscation, process injection, and kernel-level manipulation underlines their intent to achieve a higher level of stealth and persistence.

**Techniques, Tools, and Methods Discussed:**

*   **General AV/EDR Evasion:**
    *   **Code Obfuscation:**  The group focuses on creating "clean" builds, implying obfuscation or other methods to make the code harder to detect by antivirus and EDR solutions. This includes removing dependencies on certain libraries (like win32u) to reduce the signature size and complexity of the executable.
    *   **Memory Injection:** The use of in-memory DLL loading is mentioned. The technique aims to avoid writing malicious code to disk, as that is a common trigger for AV/EDR systems.
    *   **Process Injection**: "inject in kobu" is mentioned, referring to process injection. This is the act of injecting malicious code in a legitimate process to evade detections.
    *   **Sleepmasking**: It is mentioned that the 'kobu' is self protecting within its 'sleep', suggesting that the malicious code remains hidden while not actively executing.
    *   **Sleep Masking**. Several mentions of this, with specific "Sleepmask kit loaded," indicate the usage of techniques to obfuscate the code while in a dormant state, to avoid behavioral detection.
    *   **Crypters**: Several crypters are mentioned, as well as constantly trying to maintain a clean state, indicating the group is actively attempting to bypass AV/EDR.
    *   **Certificate Manipulation**: Several discussions refer to files that have to be signed, or JS requiring certificate, it would appear that this is for either code signing evasion or for evasion when using spear phishing links.
    *   **Userwx Flag**. Many mentions of this flag being enabled or disabled in relation to how much a file is detected.
    *   **Rundll32**: The command `rundll32.exe dll.dll,Enter` is often used as an execution command. This is a living-off-the-land technique used to evade detection by using legitimate system tools to execute malicious code.
    *   **File Splitting**: Multiple mentions of dropping DLL files, using them in memory, and then deleting them.
    *   **NTAPI**: The mention of rewriting to NTAPI indicates an understanding that using direct system calls, rather than higher-level Windows API functions, can help bypass certain security products. NTAPI is less monitored and therefore is a better technique for evasion.

*   **Cobalt Strike Specific:**
    *   The log contains multiple discussion regarding the configuration of Cobalt Strike and related arsenal kits for offensive operations. This would indicate that Cobalt Strike is one of their preferred method of carrying out their attack.

*   **C2 Frameworks**:
    *   The group used Cobalt Strike as one of its primary frameworks.
    *   Meta is mentioned as a separate tool, and its advantages over Cobalt Strike in the context of AV evasion are discussed.
    *   The group has developers who are changing the tool to suit their needs.

*   **AV/EDR Killers (Implied):**
    *   **"userwx" configuration:** They discuss adjusting the 'userwx' setting, possibly in the Cobalt Strike staging configuration. The exact effect depends on how it's implemented, but generally, userwx relates to memory protection, making certain memory regions writable and executable. Disabling this setting can help avoid detection by memory protection mechanisms.

*   **Specific Detections and Workarounds:**
    *   The group identifies "SearchProtocolHost.exe" creation as a trigger for dynamic detection and modify their dropper accordingly.
    *   Kaspersky EDR reportedly detects the "zakerep" technique being used by the group.

**EDR Killer Tools or Methods Discussed:**

*   The chat log does not explicitly mention the use or development of specific tools explicitly designed to kill EDR solutions. However, the high level of focus on anti-detection techniques suggests that they prioritize avoiding detection in the first place, and the use of Cobalt Strike and other offensive tools themselves might implicitly be intended to disable or bypass some security features.

**Overall:**

The group demonstrates a strong focus on AV and EDR evasion, actively working to improve their techniques and adapt to new detections. They use a combination of code obfuscation, in-memory execution, process injection, and customized tools to minimize their footprint and evade security products. They also acknowledge the arms race nature of the field and are constantly looking for new methods and better crypters to stay ahead of detection.

The chat log discusses various techniques related to EDR evasion and antivirus bypasses:

*   **General Discussions on Bypassing Detection:** The group extensively discusses whether their payloads are being detected by antivirus or EDR solutions ("палит," "детект"). They try different methods to avoid detection and work to fix or clean their tools.
*   **MSI packaging:** They are using MSI installers to try and bypass detection, noting that "палевная длка в .msi чисто" indicating that the MSI is somehow helping to avoid initial detection.
*   **Obfuscation and Runtime Cleaning:** One member mentions cleaning "динамику в дропере своем" which suggests they are using techniques to hide malicious code during runtime.
*   **Code Signing:** The group uses code signing in their build process, as implied by the filenames containing "signed_". The use of a valid signature helps in bypassing AV/EDR solutions that may not fully inspect files from a trusted signer.
*   **Traffic Analysis:** Mention of "чистота IP" alludes to concerns about traffic analysis or reputation-based blocking.
*   **UserWX Setting:** A setting `userwx "false"`is discussed in the context of cobalt strike and evading EDR.
*   **JS Obfuscation/Cleaning:** There are frequent mentions of JS files and the need to clean them to remove known signatures. The PikaBot trojan is a recurring detection, highlighting the effort required to avoid this signature.
*   **Shellcode Injection:** They are using reflective DLL injection techniques, and also trying to modify this reflective DLL, seemingly to evade current EDR detections.
*   **UDRL:** There is frequent mention of using and modifying the UDRL (user defined reflective loader) in Cobalt Strike to evade detections. They're seeking to make use of newer ones, and complaining that it's causing run time detection.
*  **"Dropper" usage and detection:** They mention that their 'dropper' is being detected and actively work to avoid this by reconfiguring the process.
*  **"C2 Obfustication"**: the group discusses about API calls being "revered and automated", to the point where the tool used to generate the malware, cobalt strike, could be DDoS'd by reverse engineers. There is also discussion of using 'DNS over HTTPS'
*  **Threathlocker Detection:** it's mentioned that endpoints protected by Threathlocker are blocking "exe" execution, forcing them to run DLL execution.

Based on the above points, the group is actively engaged in creating and using techniques to evade EDR and antivirus solutions. The group's focus is on code obfuscation (JS and DLL), using MSI installers to bypass security, and IP address management to evade reputation-based detection. There's also an ongoing effort to keep their toolset, particularly the Cobalt Strike framework, functional against evolving detection methods.

**Detailed Description of Techniques, Tools, or Methods:**

1.  **Symantec Removal:**
    *   The group discusses downloading a Symantec removal tool (`https://www.softlay.com/downloads/symantec-cleanwipe-removal-tool`).
    *   The tool consists of two files `CleanWipe.exe` and `CleanWipe.db`.
    *   This implies an effort to completely remove Symantec antivirus, likely to facilitate successful exploitation.
2.  **Symantec Tampering:**
    *   Mention is made of disabling Tamper Protection within Symantec and ultimately disabling Symantec entirely to prevent detection.
3.  **Exclusion of Files/Directories in Security Software:**
    *   The chat mentions adding files and entire directories (specifically the C: drive) to the exclusion lists of security products.  Specific examples include 'Security Risk', 'Sonar', 'DNS', 'APP', Application Exceptions, and DNS Exceptions. This is to prevent scanning and detection of malicious files.
4.  **"Boku" vs. "Elusive" Builders:**
    *   They are discussing two different payload builders: "Boku" and "Elusive". "Boku" is suggested as a replacement for "Elusive", but has compatibility issues. Cob is working on this builder.
5.  **Payload Obfuscation and Anti-Analysis:**
    *   A user ("cob_crypt_ward") is working on updating a loader with anti-analysis features:
        *   **Legitimate Resource Insertion:** Adding resources from legitimate files (e.g., OneDrive) to make the payload appear benign.
        *   **PE Section Cloning:** Cloning sections from legitimate PE files (e.g., .bss) and setting them to read-only.
        *   **Import Table Cloning:** Cloning the import table (IAT) to match legitimate files.
        *   **Executable Address Table (EAT):**  Adding the EAT, possibly to further mimic legitimate files and confuse analysis.
        *   **File Size Padding:** Increasing the file size to match the original legitimate file, likely to avoid suspicious small file detection.
        *   **DLL Hollowing:** Implementing DLL hollowing to inject shellcode into a legitimate DLL, potentially bypassing static analysis.
    *   YARA signature cleaning as part of the process.
6.  **EDR Testing:**
    *   Testing payloads against specific EDR and AV solutions and listing detection success.

The following content from the chat log relates to EDR evasion techniques, antivirus bypasses, or EDR killer tools/methods:

*   **Mention of Zscaler:** Zscaler is a cloud-based security platform that provides a range of security services, including secure web gateway, firewall, and intrusion prevention. The threat actors mention that "zscaller выебал" which translates to "Zscaler fucked [us]". They also mention that Zscaler added a rule automatically, blocking communication between bots and the administration panel and generating alerts when they tried to use "коба" (possibly referring to Cobalt Strike, a penetration testing tool). This indicates that Zscaler detected and blocked their malicious activity. They also mention facing Zscaler previously when trying to exfiltrate data with `rclone`, and seeing alerts in the console about it.
*   **"Fire Eye выебал всех" - "Fire Eye fucked everyone":** This indicates FireEye is present and causing problems for the group, suggesting that FireEye detected their activity. The group also references the automated changing of the administrator password after they logged in to multiple machines.
*   **"Криптер придет" - "Crypter will come":** This refers to the use of a crypter, which is used to obfuscate malware code to avoid detection by antivirus software. The threat actors initially believed a crypter would help bypass detection. The group members discussed the need for a "криптер" (crypter) to obfuscate malicious files. Although it's later dismissed as the problem was firewall related ("было дело не в крипте"), the initial expectation of using a crypter to bypass security measures points to an AV/EDR evasion attempt.
*   **"через носок" - "through a sock":** This refers to using a SOCKS proxy to mask their IP address and potentially evade detection. The threat actors used SOCKS proxies to "крутил" (rotated) connections, potentially for reconnaissance or lateral movement within the network. They initially used SOCKS successfully to perform actions like dumping Kerberos tickets ("кербы снять"), cracking them ("раскурить"), and scanning the network. However, the SOCKS proxy was later added to a blocklist, indicating that the security system detected and blocked the malicious traffic originating from the proxy.
*   **Adding Domain to Whitelist (Circumventing Firewall):** To bypass DNS restrictions and firewall blocks, one threat actor used social engineering to get a domain whitelisted by the target's support team, thus allowing botnet communication.
*   **"у тебя есть чистый серт для подписи dll?" - "do you have a clean certificate for signing dll?":** This suggests that the actors are interested in digitally signing their DLLs, which can help bypass security measures because trusted digital signatures are often considered as indicators of legitimate software.
*   **"закрепа оставил которые сказал не найдут" - "left foothold that he said they wont find":** The actor mentions planting persistence mechanisms ("закрепа") that are hard to detect, suggesting they have methods to maintain access to the target system even after being discovered initially.
*   **"Кербов куча есть и хеши дцшек есть" - "There are many kerbs and hashes of dcs":** Dumps of Kerberos tickets and Domain Controller password hashes are often used in post-exploitation activities, to perform lateral movement or privilege escalation without triggering alerts, by impersonating legitimate users, avoiding traditional password-based authentication.

In summary, the group discusses various techniques to evade detection including obfuscation with crypters, masking their IP address with SOCKS proxies, social engineering to whitelist malicious traffic, persistence mechanisms, using valid credentials, and exploiting SQL injection. They discuss challenges presented by FireEye and Zscaler, indicating awareness of these specific security solutions and their impact on their operations.

    *   `@usernamenn` mentions "PTH and make\_token not relevant." These are techniques used to pass-the-hash and create impersonation tokens respectively, but the user says they are not effective in the target environment. This is relevant to the topic because these methods are frequently monitored by EDR and other endpoint security solutions. The inability to use them suggests the security posture is configured to specifically prevent these tactics.
