**Detailed Description:**

1.  **esxiscan with bypass ping:** This tool likely attempts to scan ESXi servers while actively trying to evade detection. The phrase "bypass ping" hints that it uses a specific method to prevent its initial network probe from being logged or triggering alerts from network or endpoint security systems, which may involve using different port or protocol or crafting the probe to look as legitimate traffic.

2.  **Pass-the-Hash and Make Token Ineffectiveness:** The lack of effectiveness of Pass-the-Hash (PTH) and Make Token attacks suggests robust endpoint security measures are in place. Pass-the-hash is a technique where an attacker uses the hash of a user's password to authenticate to a system, instead of the password itself. "Make token" refers to creating impersonation tokens, which are used to impersonate a user. The ineffectiveness indicates the system is configured to block these attacks. This likely means the target environment has enabled protections like Credential Guard or similar mitigations against credential theft and reuse. The use of Kerberos Tickets only, further indicates security measures against legacy NTLM protocols and PTH.

**NOTE:** The Kerberos tickets dumps (`$krb5tgs$...`) are not directly EDR evasion techniques, but are related to the post-exploitation phase. The attacker successfully dumped Kerberos tickets and is now using them to move laterally within the network. They are not directly EDR evasion methods, but they are indicative of the attacker's ability to access credentials and move laterally.

BRC4 is suggested as a potential replacement or supplementary tool.

In summary, the conversation revolves around using crypters and file signing to bypass antivirus and EDR solutions, including Windows Defender, Trend Micro and CrowdStrike. They are also exploring alternative tools like BRC4 as Cobalt Strike becomes more easily detected.

**Analysis:**

*   The group is discussing techniques to evade **Sentinel**, which is likely referring to Microsoft Sentinel, a cloud-native SIEM (Security Information and Event Management) and SOAR (Security Orchestration, Automation, and Response) solution that often integrates with EDR solutions.
*   They are using **cryptors** to obfuscate their malware (`cob33_DNS_x86.bin`, `cob33_DNS_x64.bin`) to bypass detection. The file extensions suggest these are DNS-related binaries.
*   They are **signing** their crypted binaries, likely to bypass signature-based detection or to appear as legitimate software.
*   They are facing issues where their previous attempts at crypting for Sentinel have failed, indicating that Sentinel is detecting the malware despite their efforts. The message "Вчерашний крипт подписанный не дает запускать Sentinel, но и файлы exe и dll не удаляет после запуска" implies that Sentinel might be preventing execution but not removing the files (possibly due to insufficient permissions or other constraints).
*   `burito` is using **`rundll32.exe dll.dll,Reflective`**. This command suggests the use of a reflective DLL injection technique. `rundll32.exe` is a legitimate Windows utility used to execute DLLs. By injecting malicious code into `rundll32.exe`, they are attempting to execute their code in a trusted process, which can help bypass some security controls. The `Reflective` part of the command suggests that the injected DLL is performing reflective loading, where it manually loads itself into memory without relying on the Windows loader, potentially bypassing certain detection mechanisms.
*   `burito` successfully made a x86 version, signed it and sent it in a 7z archive named `signed_BD497BB84B.7z`
*   x64 cryptor version "crashes" so he will try again.

The chat log contains information relevant to EDR evasion techniques.

**Details:**

*   **GraphStrike (RedSiege/GraphStrike):** The chat log mentions "https://github.com/RedSiege/GraphStrike" multiple times. The description accompanying the link indicates that GraphStrike is a toolset that allows Cobalt Strike's HTTPS beacon to utilize the Microsoft Graph API for C2 communication. All beacon traffic is transmitted through two files created on the attacker's SharePoint site, and all messages from the beacon are routed to `https://graph.microsoft.com`.

    *   **Evasion Technique:** This is an EDR evasion technique because it disguises C2 traffic as legitimate Microsoft Graph API traffic. By using a trusted Microsoft service (SharePoint and Graph API), the tool attempts to blend in with normal network activity, making it harder for EDR solutions to detect malicious communication based on network patterns. This hides the beacon activity within a high volume of legitimate traffic, a common bypass technique.

*   **@n3auxaxl (2024-02-03 17:22:33):** "Привет, все получается, до сих пор работаю, полностью избавился от алгоримтов AES + Base64, переделал все на Bytes Structure + RC4 + кастомный алгоритм перестановок, работаю 24/7 чтобы все было готово к понедельнику и наконец то на бота без багов запустились"

    *   **Description:** This message indicates a significant effort to obfuscate and encrypt the bot's code to evade detection. Specifically, the user is:
        *   Removing AES and Base64, likely because they are easily identified by security software.
        *   Replacing them with a "Bytes Structure," RC4 encryption, and a custom permutation algorithm. This aims to create a more unique and less recognizable encryption scheme. The goal is to prevent reverse engineers from quickly analyzing the code and also to bypass signature-based detection.
*   **@n3auxaxl (2024-02-03 21:11:50):** "доделываю еще по боту фичи, защищаю все по максимум, чтобы реврсеры не пролезали"

    *   **Description:** The message indicates that additional features are being added to the bot to make it more difficult to reverse engineer and analyze. This implies anti-debugging, anti-VM, or other techniques to hinder analysis.
*   **@n3auxaxl (2024-02-03 21:25:05):** "msi, msix больше не работает обход, его отрубили"
    *   **Description:** This reveals an attempt to bypass security measures using the `MSIX` installer format. The fact that the "obход" (bypass) is no longer working indicates that security vendors have likely patched the vulnerability or implemented detections for this technique. They are pivoting to MSI as the installer.
*   **@ssd (2024-02-03 21:26:08):** "govoat msi 15-20 dowland i palitsa"
    *   **Description:** This message suggests that MSI-based payloads are getting flagged (палиться) after 15-20 downloads. This means that signature-based detections or reputation-based systems are likely identifying the files after a certain number of executions.
*   **@n3auxaxl (2024-02-03 21:27:01):** "я могу сделать 100 билдов уник и выдавать 1 файл 15 раз"

    *   **Description:** The user proposes a technique to address the issue of MSI files getting flagged. They plan to create 100 unique builds of the malware and distribute each build only 15 times before switching to a new build. This is a classic approach to evade signature-based detection and reputation-based systems. By creating many unique files, it becomes more difficult for security vendors to create a single signature that identifies all variants. Also the downloads will be less than the threshold needed to be "palitsa"
*   **General talk around bot security:** There are also mentions on securing "bot" to not have bugs, this can be indirectly related to security evasion as a more stable bot would also imply better detection evasion.

**Summary of Techniques Discussed:**

1.  **Custom Encryption and Obfuscation:** Replacing standard encryption algorithms (AES, Base64) with custom schemes (Bytes Structure, RC4, permutation algorithms) to hinder analysis and evade signature-based detection.
2.  **Anti-Reverse Engineering:** Adding features to the bot that make it harder to reverse engineer, like anti-debugging or anti-VM techniques.
3.  **MSI Installer Bypass:** Exploiting vulnerabilities in MSI installer to execute malicious code.
4.  **Polymorphism (Unique Builds):** Generating numerous unique builds of the malware and distributing each build only a limited number of times to evade signature-based detection and reputation systems.

The group discusses techniques to evade EDR/AV solutions. They are considering:
*   **Process Hollowing:** This is a known technique where a legitimate process is started in a suspended state, its memory is unmapped, and malicious code is written into it.
*   **CreateRemoteThread:** This is a Windows API call that allows a thread to be created in another process, effectively injecting code into that process.  The group seems to prefer this technique because it is less likely to be detected.

N3auxaxl mentions that *Process Hollow* can be detected, because it's a well known and commonly used, therefore overexposed/worn out technique. However they mention that *CreateRemoteThread* should be preferred as a primary injection method. They also mention that if there are no processes to inject, *process hollow* has to be used.

They attempt to test an EXE file, however it is caught as soon as its signature makes a connection, suggesting the EDR solution was in fact capable of detecting and mitigating its presence, and connection.

The following content relates to potential EDR/AV evasion or disabling based on the chat log:

*   **Discussion of blocked execution:** At `2024-02-24 09:38:52`, `@lapa` states "думаю как раз потому, что там заблочили запуск" which translates to "I think precisely because they blocked the launch there."  This suggests they encountered a system where their initial execution attempts were blocked, potentially by an EDR or AV solution.

*   **Samba Share Redirection:** From `2024-02-24 10:46:02` to `2024-02-24 11:21:23`, `@lapa` details a technique to deliver payloads via Samba share redirection:

    *   Embed a Samba (SMB) link in a PDF or HTML file.  If the PDF is blocked (e.g., by Outlook), the SMB link can be delivered within an HTML file and sent via other methods.  The HTML redirects to the SMB link.
    *   Bundle the HTML file in a ZIP archive along with "junk" files to potentially bypass filters.
    *   Automate the creation of Samba links.
    *   Automatically collect logs of hashes from all Samba links.

    **Technique Analysis:** This method leverages SMB to capture NetNTLM hashes. Delivery through a ZIP containing junk files and an HTML redirector is used to bypass initial email security checks.  The automation of Samba link creation and hash collection shows a systematic approach to credential harvesting. The discussion implies they're aiming to exploit the automatic NTLM authentication in Windows when accessing SMB shares. This could bypass AV/EDR systems that don't deeply inspect SMB traffic.

*   **New Exchange Exploit:** At `2024-02-24 12:11:29`, `@usernamegg` mentions "это с нового сплойта для экчейнжей" which translates to "this is from a new exploit for Exchange." This likely refers to exploiting a newly discovered vulnerability in Microsoft Exchange to gain access to systems. While not directly about evasion, exploiting vulnerabilities is often a precursor to needing evasion techniques.

Based on the provided chat log, here's the extracted content related to EDR evasion, antivirus bypasses, and EDR killer tools/methods:

    *   `[2024-02-26 09:56:17] @lapa:matrix.bestflowers247.online: да`

    *   `[2024-02-26 09:56:42] @lapa:matrix.bestflowers247.online: хватит уже`
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:58:02] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: я js не буду слать`
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:58:06] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: хватит уже`
    *   This conversation indicates JavaScript (.js) files are being detected more frequently ( "мы уже сколько его шлем") and are therefore avoided.

*   **Use of DLLs for Evasion**
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:53:12] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: и скину в общий чат к криптерам`
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:53:31] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: Давай dll сделаю, я думаю она лучше будет идти`
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:53:45] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: из под JS можно запускать без палева через regsvr32 точно`
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:57:54] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: Запустил билд DLL под regsvr32 или rundll32 file.dll, DllRegisterServer или DllInstall или DllUnregisterServer`
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:58:31] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: имею ввиду, что может с DLL все намного лучше будет`
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:58:40] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: ибо она не такая палевная при дрпое на диск как EXE`
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:59:39] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: ибо DLL надо запускать через что то, по типу regsvr32 или rundll32, а это от microsoft же и его сложнее задетектить, а когда 2 раза кликнул, то АВ легче намного понять что делать`
    *   This highlights the use of DLLs (Dynamic Link Libraries) as a method to evade detection.
        *   **Reasoning:** DLLs, when executed via legitimate system utilities like `regsvr32` and `rundll32`, may be less likely to raise AV/EDR suspicion initially compared to standalone executables (.exe).  The fact that DLLs require a legitimate host process makes them appear less suspicious.
        *   **Techniques:**
            *   **regsvr32**: Used to register DLLs as COM (Component Object Model) objects. By registering a malicious DLL, its code can be executed.  This is often used because `regsvr32` is a signed Microsoft binary and therefore may be whitelisted or less scrutinized by security products.
            *   **rundll32**: Used to execute specific functions within a DLL.  Similar to `regsvr32`, `rundll32` is a signed Microsoft binary.
    *   `[2024-02-26 11:56:30] @burito:matrix.bestflowers247.online: rundll32.exe dll.dll,DllRegisterServer` provides a specific command line example.

*   **Process Injection:**
    *   `[2024-02-26 11:56:30] @burito:matrix.bestflowers247.online: dr1_inject.dll - это с инжектом в процесс, надо бы тестануть как работать будет`
    *   `[2024-02-26 12:58:46] @burito:matrix.bestflowers247.online: ладно. все равно палится будет дефом, там инжект обычный. на других может сработает`
    *   This describes a DLL `dr1_inject.dll` specifically designed for process injection.  Process injection involves inserting malicious code into the address space of another (typically legitimate) running process, which then executes that code.  This is used to hide malicious activity and evade detection, as the injected code appears to be part of the trusted process.
        *   The group acknowledges that "обычный" (basic) process injection can be detected by "дефом" (presumably a shortened form of "defender," likely referring to Windows Defender or a similar AV product).

*   **Use of Crypters**
    *   `[2024-02-26 10:28:59] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: Уже отдал если что на крипт и все пролил`
    *   `[2024-02-26 12:05:45] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: кароче да, инждект почему то не отработал у тебя, а дефолтный крипт спалило`
    *   `[2024-02-26 12:29:20] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: от липиса крипт если что чситый`
    *   The log mentions using "крипт" (crypt), likely referring to a crypter. A crypter is a tool designed to obfuscate malicious code (executables, DLLs, etc.) to make them harder for anti-virus and other security software to detect. Crypters often use encryption and other techniques to change the code's signature, thus bypassing signature-based detection methods.
    *    `[2024-02-26 19:19:11] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: у тебя крипт не работает на тредмикро`
    *    `[2024-02-26 19:19:16] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: почему не можешь обойти ?`
    *   They have issues with evading TrendMicro

*   **Fileless execution**
    *   `[2024-02-26 09:53:45] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: из под JS можно запускать без палева через regsvr32 точно`
    *   The "из под JS можно запускать без палева через regsvr32 точно" means that they are using JS to execute `regsvr32` which helps them achieve Fileless execution.

**Description:**

This describes a DLL side-loading technique to bypass detection. A malicious DLL replaces a legitimate DLL used by 7-Zip. The legitimate 7-Zip executable is launched, which in turn loads and executes the malicious DLL. This allows malicious code to run within a trusted process, potentially evading detection based on process reputation. The malicious DLL contains dropper functionality, designed to then inject into further processes. The whole process is packaged as an MSI installer.

The chat log contains multiple discussions related to EDR evasion, antivirus bypasses, and related tools and techniques. Here's a breakdown:

*   **AV KILLER Discussion (@usernamegg):** This is a detailed discussion about AV/EDR killer tools and methods.
    *   Techniques:
        *   **Kernel Code Execution:** The core of most AV killers involves running code in the kernel.
        *   **Kernel Exploits:** Leveraging vulnerabilities in the kernel.
        *   **Signed Driver Exploits:** Exploiting vulnerabilities or functionality within signed drivers. This involves abusing signed drivers to gain kernel-level privileges.
        *   **Own Signed Drivers:** Developing and using their own signed drivers for kernel access.
    *   References/Links:
        *   `https://www.aon.com/cyber-solutions/aon_cyber_labs/yours-truly-signed-av-driver-weaponizing-an-antivirus-driver/`
        *   `https://rce4fun.blogspot.com/2018/02/malwarefox-antimalware-zam64sys.html`
        *   `https://www.trendmicro.com/en_th/research/22/e/avoslocker-ransomware-variant-abuses-driver-file-to-disable-anti-Virus-scans-log4shell.html`
        *   `https://synzack.github.io/Blinding-EDR-On-Windows/`
    *   Lists of Drivers (Potentially Vulnerable):
        *   `https://github.com/eclypsium/Screwed-Drivers/blob/master/DRIVERS.md`
        *   `https://github.com/CaledoniaProject/drivers-binaries`
    *   Microsoft's Reaction: Mentions Microsoft's driver block rules (`https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/security/application-security/application-control/windows-defender-application-control/design/microsoft-recommended-driver-block-rules`).

*   **Sentinel Bypass Discussion:** Several exchanges revolve around bypassing SentinelOne (an EDR solution).

- **ESXi Exploitation and Defense Evasion**
    - **esxcli system settings advanced set -o /User/execInstalledOnly -i 0:** This command, typically used in ESXi environments, attempts to disable a security setting ("execInstalledOnly") that restricts the execution of unsigned or untrusted code. Setting the value to "0" effectively allows the execution of code from any source, which could potentially bypass security controls or detection mechanisms.
    - **nohup sh -c '/tmp/encryptor -killesxi' > /dev/null 2>&1 &:** This command suggests an attempt to terminate ESXi processes. "nohup" ensures the process continues running even if the user disconnects. "sh -c" executes the command string. The command implies that an executable named `/tmp/encryptor`, possibly a custom tool, is being run with the "-killesxi" argument, likely to kill ESXi processes. ```text
- Discussion of "обходилки сентиля" ("bypass Sentinel"), implying the need to circumvent the SentinelOne EDR product. This indicates interest in evading EDR.

- "esxcli system settings advanced set -o /User/execInstalledOnly -i 0" command.  This command, when executed on an ESXi host, is used to disable the "execInstalledOnly" advanced setting. Disabling this setting allows the execution of unsigned or non-VIB packaged code on the ESXi host.  This is a common technique used by attackers to bypass security controls and execute malicious code within the virtualized environment. The chat also discusses the effectiveness of this command on different ESXi versions.

- Mentions of "крипт сделать*" "криптанем локер ?", the use of crypters and locker, to evade anti-virus
```

**Detailed Description of the Techniques, Tools, or Methods Discussed:**

1.  **Bypassing SentinelOne EDR:** The mention of "обходилки сентиля" shows that the group is interested in bypassing the SentinelOne EDR. This suggests that they either have already developed a technique to do so, or are looking for one.

2.  **Disabling ESXi Host Security Setting:**
    *   **Command:** `esxcli system settings advanced set -o /User/execInstalledOnly -i 0`
    *   **Purpose:** This ESXi command disables a security setting that restricts the execution of code to only VIB-packaged or signed code. By disabling this setting, attackers can execute arbitrary, unsigned code directly on the ESXi host, circumventing a layer of security control.
    *   **Context:** The chat participants discuss the command's effectiveness on different ESXi versions (specifically 7 and 8) and troubleshoot why it might not be working. The discussion implies they are using this command to facilitate malicious activities on compromised ESXi hosts.
    *   **Why it's relevant to EDR/AV evasion:** Disabling `execInstalledOnly` removes a key restriction on code execution within the virtualized environment, making it easier to deploy and run malware undetected. This is because the malware doesn't need to be properly signed or packaged, which would normally raise red flags for security solutions.

3.  **Using crypters and lockers**
    *   **Goal:** Evade detection by antivirus or EDR solutions by encrypting or obfuscating malicious code.
    *   **Context:** It's briefly mentioned in the context of "крипт сделать*" "криптанем локер ?" the usage of a crypter will potentially allow successful deployment of the locker.

Description of techniques, tools, or methods discussed:

1.  **7zG.exe Antivirus Bypass:** This technique attempts to leverage the 7-Zip executable (7zG.exe) to bypass antivirus detection. The specifics of the method are not detailed in the log snippet, but it likely involves bundling a malicious payload within a 7-Zip archive and using 7zG.exe to extract and execute it. The intent is for the AV to trust the legitimate 7-Zip binary and thus not scan the malicious payload within the archive during execution.
2.  **SentinelOne EDR Bypass Attempt:** A failed attempt to bypass SentinelOne EDR is mentioned, which indicates the group is targeting this specific EDR solution. The specific technique used to bypass SentinelOne is not described in this log.
3.  **DLL/EXE Naming Scheme:** This technique involves keeping the DLL file name consistent while randomizing or changing the EXE file name to avoid detection. This could be based on the assumption that EDR/AV systems are more sensitive to DLL file names than EXE names.
4.  **PE Section Spoofer:** This involves randomizing PE sections, where a PE (Portable Executable) file is the standard format for executables, object code, DLLs and others used in Windows operating systems. Randomizing PE sections is used to change the structure and characteristics of the executable, making it more difficult for antivirus and EDR solutions to identify the file based on static signatures or common patterns.
5.  **Crypter with Rundll32 Execution:** A crypter is being used to obfuscate a DLL. The `rundll32` command is then used to execute the DLL, which helps bypass some AV/EDR systems that may have heuristics for commonly used executable names. The "FIlojmZCAsZI" part of "rundll32 dll.dll,FIlojmZCAsZI" is most likely the function that is being called inside the dll.
6.  **Rundll32 with file.dll and EppBRSetCallback:** Again, the use of Rundll32 is used to execute the file.dll, in addition to the function EppBRSetCallback.

Here's the analysis of the chat log for content related to EDR evasion techniques, antivirus bypasses, and EDR killer tools or methods:

**1. Process Injection/Infection:**

*   **[2024-03-28 15:26:13] @muaddib6:matrix.org: Делаю инфецированием** This indicates they are creating payloads with process injection (or process "infection"). This is a common technique to bypass security products by injecting malicious code into a legitimate, trusted process.

**Description of EDR/AV Evasion Techniques:**

The threat actors are actively working on making their malware more difficult to detect by EDR/AV solutions.
*   **Polymorphism:**  `@n3auxaxl` is working on creating polymorphic builds, meaning each build of the malware will have a different code signature. This makes signature-based detection less effective.
*   **Dynamic Hashes & Keys:**  They are changing the hashes and keys used within the malware to be dynamic, using WinAPI.  This means that the hashes and keys are generated at runtime, making static analysis more difficult.
*   **Code Morphing:** `@n3auxaxl` is using a system for morphing the code of their tool to protect it from reverse engineering and signature detection.
*   **Avoiding Static Signatures:**  The goal is to prevent EDR/AV solutions from easily creating static signatures for their malware.
*   **Obfuscation and Anti-Reversing:** The group is focused on increasing the level of effort required to reverse engineer their malware.

The chat log indicates a clear understanding of how EDR/AV solutions work and active development to bypass these protections.

[2024-04-07]
----------
The chat log contains one possible indicator of EDR/AV evasion:

*   **"сегодня рефлективкой займусь"**: This translates to "today I will do reflective." The term "reflective" in this context could refer to Reflective DLL Injection. This is a technique used to load a DLL into a process's memory from memory rather than from disk. This is often used to bypass AV/EDR as the DLL is never written to disk, making detection more difficult.

**Detailed description of the technique:**

Reflective DLL injection is a method of loading a dynamic-link library (DLL) into a process's memory without the need for traditional loading mechanisms (i.e., not from the disk). Instead, the DLL is loaded directly from memory (e.g., from an encoded string or downloaded resource). This method is often used to circumvent security measures that rely on monitoring file system access or DLL loading events. The technique typically involves the following steps:

1.  **DLL Payload:** A malicious DLL is prepared, which contains the code to be injected.

2.  **Loader Code:** A piece of code (the "loader") is created. This loader's main function is to allocate memory within the target process, copy the DLL into that memory, and then resolve the DLL's imports and relocations so it can function correctly. It essentially mimics the actions of the operating system's DLL loader but does it entirely in memory.

3.  **Injection:** The loader code is injected into the target process (using techniques like process injection).

4.  **Execution:** Once injected, the loader code executes, which in turn loads the DLL from memory and runs its entry point.

By loading the DLL reflectively, it bypasses many common security measures that monitor file system activity and standard DLL loading mechanisms. It's a popular technique used by attackers to deliver payloads stealthily.

**Summary**

This chat log shows a cybercrime group discussing and using various techniques aimed at bypassing security controls. These techniques include:

*   **Exploiting vulnerabilities:** Using CVE-2024-21378 to gain initial access and execute code.
*   **Payload obfuscation:** Encrypting shellcode to avoid signature-based detection.
*   **Process injection:** Injecting shellcode into other processes to mask malicious activity.
*   **In-memory execution:** Executing code directly from memory to avoid writing files to disk, which would be subject to scanning.
*   **Reverse Engineering/Understanding Tools:** Reverse engineering tools they use to understand how it works to potentially bypass or improve their exploitation efforts.
*   **Anti-Detect Browsers** Use of "брейкер" (breaker). These tools attempt to fingerprinting, isolation, and other techniques to evade detection.
*  **C2 infrastructure:** Reliance on their own C2 infrastructure "пику" to mask network traffic.
* C2 Profiling: Changing Cobalt Strike profiles and configuration to evade detection.

**Detailed Description of Techniques, Tools, or Methods Discussed:**

*   **Crypters:** Software used to obfuscate malicious code (DLLs in this case) to avoid detection by antivirus and EDR systems. They work by encrypting or otherwise modifying the code to change its signature and make it appear benign.
*   **DLL Injection/Execution via `rundll32.exe`:** A technique to execute malicious code by injecting a DLL into a legitimate process (using `rundll32.exe`). This can help bypass AV/EDR systems that primarily focus on executable files. Specifying a function to execute (e.g., `GetModuleProp`) allows the DLL's functionality to be invoked.
*   **0-Click Exploits:** Exploits that execute without any user interaction beyond opening an email or document. In this case, they exploit a vulnerability in Outlook to execute code automatically.
*   **Kernel-Mode EDR Killer:** A tool designed to disable or bypass EDR functionality by operating at the kernel level of the operating system. Such tools usually require administrator privileges and can potentially compromise system security if successful.
*  **Anti-Analysis (Dynamic Analysis Evasion)** Techniques to make it difficult to analyze the malware's behavior during runtime.

The chat log contains the following content related to EDR evasion and antivirus bypass techniques:

*   **SmartScreen bypass:** User `cob_crypt_ward` describes a method of bypassing SmartScreen using a chain of actions initiated by a user visiting a URL. This prompts File Explorer to ask to open a file, a fake download page is shown and if user runs the file a payload is executed, bypassing SmartScreen without the need for certificates or DLL side-loading. The method involves luring a user to download and execute a payload that gets past the MOTW (Mark of the Web) alert, thus infecting the machine if the user clicks "Run". Screenshots were also shared in `html prompt2.PNG`, `html prompt.PNG`, and `explorer.PNG`.

*   **"своей цве" method to bypass Edge and other browsers alerts**: A user (cob\_crypt\_ward) mentions that an acquaintance created a method to bypass alerts in Edge and other browsers, described as "что-то в духе своей цве" ("something in the style of his цве"). While vague, this implies the existence of a custom-developed browser bypass technique. It is acknowledged to involve "костыли" ("workarounds").

*   **Payload customization and testing**: The group discusses building and testing payloads (`.bin` files) after encrypting them. This includes specifying the entry point `GetModuleProp` for the payload. They seem to be employing a "крипт" (encryption) and "чек" (check/test) workflow, indicating an effort to obfuscate or evade detection. There is also discussion around x86 and x64 versions of the payloads. "чисто под дефом, вроде как стучит" - suggests testing the payload under controlled conditions, possibly with a default/basic security configuration to assess its effectiveness in bypassing defenses.

*   **File Infection**: `с инфицированием + exe` - A user mentions performing file infection. The purpose would be to run malicious code with a seemingly legitimate file or application.

Based on the information provided, the following techniques are suggested to be in use:

*   **Social Engineering/User Interaction**: The SmartScreen bypass and browser alert bypass techniques heavily rely on user interaction to execute the payload, indicating a reliance on social engineering tactics.
*   **Payload Obfuscation/Encryption**: The use of "крипт" and checking of `.bin` files implies payload encryption or obfuscation techniques.

*   **Malicious File Delivery**: The chain described in the SmartScreen bypass is designed to deliver a malicious file to a user and trick them into executing it.

*   **`[2024-04-29 07:09:48] @n3auxaxl:matrix.collectionofmanager.space: он при билде кажджый раз разный получяается`**

**Details:**

*   The user `@n3auxaxl` is discussing an "x64 крипт" (x64 cryptor). Cryptors are used to obfuscate malicious code to avoid detection by antivirus (AB) and EDR systems.
*   The key statement is that "АВ и Едрки должны молчать" which translates to "AV and EDR should be silent." This indicates the goal of the cryptor is to bypass these security measures.
*   The build is "полиморфный" (polymorphic), meaning that the resulting executable changes its code with each compilation. This technique is used to evade signature-based detection by AV/EDR. Each build will have a different signature making it harder to detect with static analysis.
*   The build process produces a different output "кажджый раз разный получяается" (each time it turns out different), confirming the polymorphism.

Here's an analysis of the chat log, extracting content related to EDR evasion and AV bypass techniques:

**1. AMSI Bypass:**
*   `[2024-05-05 03:52:49] @usernamenn:matrix.bestflowers247.online: https://github.com/V-i-x-x/AMSI-BYPASS`
*   This message shares a GitHub repository link related to AMSI (Anti-Malware Scan Interface) bypass. AMSI is a Windows interface that allows applications and services to request malware scans of data. Bypassing it is a common technique for attackers to prevent their payloads from being detected by antivirus and EDR solutions.

**2. Embedding Executables in Images:**
*   `[2024-05-05 09:01:48] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: он хочет сделать обманку и вшить в картинку ехе`
*   `[2024-05-05 09:01:50] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: криптованный`
*   `[2024-05-05 09:01:53] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: сделать заупск скрытый`
*   `[2024-05-05 09:02:18] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: и им подгружется ехе от пики`
*   `[2024-05-05 09:02:28] @usernamegg:matrix.bestflowers247.online: со скрытым запуском`
*   The group is discussing embedding an executable (EXE) file within an image file. The executable is intended to be "encrypted" and run in a hidden manner. This is a classic steganography/masquerading technique to bypass initial detection.
*   Related to this, they discuss using a fake website, mimicking Fortinet, to trick users into downloading and executing the embedded file. This is a social engineering tactic.

**3. Bypassing SmartScreen:**

*   `[2024-05-05 09:04:50] @lapa:matrix.bestflowers247.online: не помогало`
    The group directly discusses bypassing SmartScreen, a Windows security feature that warns users about potentially malicious files downloaded from the internet. Their proposed methods include:
    *   **Code Signing Certificates:** "Подписать" (sign) the executable with a valid code signing certificate to increase its reputation and avoid SmartScreen warnings.
    *   **Crypting (Crypters):** Using a "хороший крипт" (good crypt) which is a crypter to obfuscate the malicious code and evade signature-based detection.
    *   "Хорошо почистить" (Clean) the executable, to remove attributes that trigger SmartScreen
*  The participants acknowledge that previous attempts at bypassing SmartScreen have failed, indicating that the evasion is not always successful.

**Description:** These messages contain commands to uninstall Tanium, an endpoint detection and response (EDR) solution, using the `msiexec.exe` tool. The `/x` flag specifies that the installation package should be uninstalled. The GUIDs within the curly braces represent the unique product codes for the Tanium client installer. `/norestart` prevents a restart after uninstall, and `/qn` specifies a quiet uninstall with no user interface. This constitutes a method to remove an EDR agent from a system.

[2024-05-18]
----------
The chat log mentions techniques related to antivirus bypasses:

*   **"сурсы хорошего лоадера, который в статике не палится"**: This refers to the source code of a good loader that is not detected by static analysis. Static analysis is a method used by antivirus and EDR solutions to examine the code of a file without executing it, looking for known malicious patterns. A loader that bypasses static analysis would be one that does not contain easily identifiable malicious signatures or patterns.

*   **"статически шеллкод чистить и морфер делать"**: This mentions cleaning shellcode statically and creating a "morpher." Cleaning shellcode refers to removing or obfuscating recognizable patterns that antivirus or EDR systems might detect during static analysis. A "morpher" suggests a tool or technique used to dynamically change the shellcode's structure or appearance, making it harder to detect by signature-based or static analysis methods. This is a form of polymorphism, a common antivirus evasion technique.

Therefore, the response is:

The chat log contains discussion on antivirus bypasses, specifically:

*   A loader that bypasses static analysis ("сурсы хорошего лоадера, который в статике не палится")
*   Cleaning shellcode statically to avoid detection.
*   Using a "morpher" to change shellcode's structure to evade signature-based detection.

Using a signed dropper with an EV certificate is a common technique used to bypass initial AV/EDR detection. EV certs establish trust and make it less likely for the initial dropper to be flagged. The modular loader would then download and execute further malicious payloads (like the HVNC) to maintain persistence and hide activity.
*   **Dropper and AV Evasion:** Further context on avoiding detection, `@usernamegg` says "да не на всех они смогу скачивать по линку", "не на всех смогу запускать ехе", followed by "короче дохера сложностей там" - reflecting the challenges of getting a malicious executable to run without being detected.
*   **Fast Flux Proxy:** The actors plan to use "линки черех фастфлюкс прокси" indicating the usage of Fast Flux DNS, which is a technique used to make it harder to track the malicious infrastructure and prevent takedowns by constantly changing the IP addresses associated with a domain.
*   **Crypter Used:** The actors often seek to "криптануть" or encrypt their malware payloads. For example, `chuck` asks Ben to "криптануть соксы?". This refers to using a crypter or protector to obfuscate the code and evade detection by antivirus and EDR solutions.

The use of a crypter implies an attempt to evade signature-based AV and behavioral EDR detections. `burito` provided a crypter, file, and "строка" (likely the password) for it, showing collaborative active avoidance of AV/EDR.
*   **Loader Encryption:** `usernamegg` wanted to криптануть "локер и лоедеро" (locker and loader), which describes a similar effort to avoid detection of both the initial payload (loader) and the ransomware encryption component.
*   **Amadey Loader:** `usernamegg` wants to encrypt "Amadey.exe амадей лоадер крипт плиз" then notes, "ооо давно его не видел )". The usage of the loader in a current attack indicates it's likely used to deploy post-exploitation tooling while avoiding AV/EDR signature matching.

**Detailed Description of Techniques/Tools/Methods:**

1.  **Crypters:** The primary technique discussed is using "crypters" (encryption/obfuscation tools) to modify the executable files and evade signature-based detection by antivirus and potentially EDR solutions. The goal is to change the file's hash and characteristics to avoid being flagged as malicious. They are using a crypter to make their "socks-0603.exe" and "socks bot" undetectable.

2.  **SOCKS Proxies:** The group frequently mentions using SOCKS proxies. While not directly EDR evasion, using proxies helps mask their origin and make attribution more difficult. The constant discussion about the quality and "liveliness" of the SOCKS implies they need reliable proxies for their operations to avoid raising suspicion from source IP addresses. The ports that SOCKS runs on are being checked (ex. port 3037).

3.  **"Пролив" (Deployment/Distribution)**: The method of deploying the executables seems to be a factor in detection. There may be something about their deployment process that is making the files more easily detected.

4.  **Code Signing:** The dialogue "под тестовый прозвон черзе кол центр подговлю аки тоже, надо с ними сработаться" and the use of "spam protect" in digital signature suggests that the group may be trying to make the files seem legitimate. The conversation focuses on ways to try to trick their targets into downloading and running the malicious code.

5.   **EDR Table:** The fact that the attackers use a Table of EDR suggests that they systematically track EDRs and methods to bypass these.

6.  **CrowdStrike Evasion**: They are having trouble evading CrowdStrike and plan to fix it.

7.  **Network Visibility Bypass**: The lack of a direct conversation about network visibility suggests that they do not have a direct answer as to how to bypass this visibility.

**Conclusion:**

The chat log contains clear evidence of the cybercrime group's efforts to evade detection by security tools. They are focused on using crypters to obfuscate their malicious executables and are aware that CrowdStrike Falcon is detecting their activity.

*   **Executable Delivery and Chrome Evasion:** A significant portion of the discussion revolves around successfully delivering an executable (`.exe`) file to a target system while bypassing Chrome's built-in security features (safe browsing). Several techniques are considered and tested:

    *   **Zipping with "Garbage" Data:** Initially, they attempt to include the executable within a ZIP archive, adding additional "garbage" or "junk" data to the archive in an effort to obfuscate the presence of the executable. This method proves unsuccessful, as Chrome is still able to detect the `.exe` even within the archive. `(2024-06-04 10:17:59, 2024-06-04 10:35:36, 2024-06-04 10:37:40)`
    *   **Code Signing:** They try signing the executable with an Extended Validation (EV) certificate, and experiment with different certificates, but Chrome still flags the file as unsafe. `(2024-06-04 08:04:19, 2024-06-04 08:47:19, 2024-06-04 09:00:19, 2024-06-04 10:36:47)`
    *   **Domain Reputation:** There's a recognition that the domain from which the executable is downloaded affects Chrome's trust assessment. Newer domains are treated with more suspicion. `(2024-06-04 10:18:32)`
    *   **Mimicking Trusted Software (Putty):**  A key idea emerges: modifying the beginning bytes of their executable to match a known and trusted application (PuTTY). The hypothesis is that Chrome examines the initial bytes and attributes more trust to files that have those bytes, assuming it is a legitimate application. `(2024-06-04 10:29:49, 2024-06-04 11:24:44, 2024-06-04 12:34:51)`
    *   **MSIX Packaging:** Consideration is given to using Microsoft's MSIX packaging format as a possible delivery method. `(2024-06-04 11:26:11)`

*   **Defense Evasion and Anti-Virus Interaction:** While they are focused on Chrome's safe browsing features, they occasionally mention interactions with traditional antivirus solutions ("Def"). It appears that some of their techniques which bypass Chrome can be detected by antivirus (Windows Defender).

*   **Exploitation of VPN RCE:** Exploitation of Checkpoint VPN RCE to get reverse shell, which allows to bypass the front EDR.
*   **Bypassing initial Chrome detections**: Discussions of adding random names to the malware build and using a loader to bypass the first check made by Chrome.
